He lives in the Netherlands with his family.\n\nxxv\nAbout the Case \nStudy Author\nRüdiger Eck  is an accomplished professional in the automotive industry \nwith a distinguished career spanning over two decades. Working for two \nGerman premium OEMs, Rüdiger has over 20 years of technical track record in global automotive production, including winning two consec -\nutive world champion titles with the Mercedes Formula 1 team. He has spearheaded various Audi teams from the early stages of digitalization in the production industry and now directs Audi’s Data and Analytics Factory for Production and Logistics, as well as overseeing Volkswagen Group data activities. With a German master’s degree (“Diplom-Ingenieur”) in mechanical engineering, Rüdiger has a strong passion for managing tech -\nnical and organizational change and bridging the gap between produc -\ntion and IT. His expertise and leadership continue to drive innovation and excellence in the automotive sector.\n\nDOI: 10.1201/9781003512776-1 1 \nIntroduction\nThe world around us is changing at an unprecedented pace, and technol -\nogy is fueling the transformation. With the Internet and the power of \nsmartphones bringing unlimited information to our fingertips, we are now amid the most phenomenal transformation. With the democratiza -\ntion of GenAI, what will differentiate an enterprise from another will no longer be the technology itself but the data used by the people, processes, and technologies, not only for a competitive edge today but also to create a future-proof, long-term impact.\nWhen we, the authors, started discussing this, drawing from cumulative \nfive decades of professional experience, we concluded that most enterprises continue to struggle to harness the power of data strategically and sustain -\nably. Most Boards and CEOs realize the power data possesses but struggle to comprehend the path to take to leverage it as a strategic asset. Their common perception is that data is a technical topic that should be handled within IT, analytics, or data offices. There is confusion about who is responsible and accountable for data. Some Boards and CEOs have taken “strategic” actions by appointing a chief data officer (CDO) or a data lead, considering it a “sil -\nver bullet” to leverage data as a strategic asset. Understandably, many CDOs and data offices struggle to deliver the expected impact. Data in most enter -\nprises continues to be seen as a by-product of people, processes, or technolo -\ngies. Technological advancements have happened in the data domain with innovative technologies; however, technologies alone do not create sufficient conditions for the needed change.\nThese observations led to the realization of this book’s core purpose: \nTo serve as a catalyst for structural change in enterprises – empowering  \n2   ◾   Data as the Fourth Pillar\nBoards and CxOs to drive a paradigm shift by elevating data as the “fourth \npillar” of their operating model, along with its people, processes, and tech -\nnology. We believe that this change would enable enterprises to develop a holistic and sustainable approach to reaping the benefits from data as a strategic asset.\nOur core purpose for this book resonated with Rüdiger Eck, head of \nData and Analytics Factory for Production and Logistics at Audi AG. They decided to come on board to provide their case study, which enriches the content with points of view and examples of an enterprise undergoing this journey. We share Rüdiger’s introduction to the case study below.\nOur book highlights the importance of data for Boards and CEOs and \nprovides a roadmap for them and their CxOs to become mature in lever -\naging data and be prepared for future innovations that leverage data as a core foundation. Our book contains real-world examples, frameworks, maturity models, Food for Thought  questions, and actionable insights \nthat should give senior executives a practical guide to apply in their enterprises.\nWe have structured the content of this book in the following chapters:\n• Chapter\xa0 1 : This chapter addresses “Why data should be treated as \nthe fourth pillar,” highlighting the potential that data provides to enterprises looking to scale their artificial intelligence (AI) initiatives and the significant challenges they face to realize that potential. This chapter presents an assessment framework to assess and align the challenges and a framework to understand the varying data intensity of use cases.\n•')

I plied my trade in \nthe financial, retail, energy and media sectors before leaping into the vendor \nworld with Zscaler. I’ve hung around on the product side for a decade with \nTanium, Contentful and now I am Chief Security Officer and Chief Product \nOfficer at Cyberhaven.\nAs a CSO, I am a trusted advisor to executives, board members and other \nstakeholders, helping them define well-balanced strategies for managing risk \nand improving business outcomes. In recent years, I have been fortunate \nenough to provide advice and opinions to start-up companies in the security \nspace, something that would not be possible without the muscle memory \nfrom my experiences in the aforementioned awesome companies.\nI hold an MSc in Cyber Security from Royal Holloway and retain an  \nactive role in the cybersecurity industry through fellowship of the Chartered \nInstitute of Security Professionals (CIISec) and various board advisory/ \ndesign partner roles.\nIn terms of socials, I am most active on Twitter/X. In 2017, I convinced my wife that a foray into published \nwriting would be a one-off. No one will care what I have to say about  \ncybersecurity, much less my tedious analogies and English humour. What do \nI know? As unlikely to me as Leicester City winning the Premier League, \nCyber Risk Management did relatively well, and I am sitting here in 2023, \ncoffee in hand, writing an intro to the second edition.\nWhy a new edition? Primarily because the breakneck pace of technology \nchange continues and security teams need to stay abreast of how to secure \nnew technologies. Security incidents have occurred over the past five years \nthat illustrate we still have a long way to go before we can consider our -\nselves ‘secure by design’. I have tried to include fresh content that identifies \nnew threat vectors, profound shifts in societal opinions or major technology \nchanges.\nI did not want to write a perfunctory update. If a job is worth doing, it is \nworth doing properly. Where I reference Large Language Models, container \nregistry solutions or shifts in government policy, I have tried to highlight the \nsecurity implications of these changes. Many of the matters I have added \nthis time are issues that affect me as a practising security officer.\nAs I outlined in the first edition of this book, being paid to write and \nspeak about my views on cybersecurity is humbling. To say that writing a \nnew version is surreal is a gross understatement. Thanks to everyone who \nbought the first edition. Thanks for all of the feedback I have received; it \nserved as motivation for the new content.\nI write based on my experiences. Many of the concepts I am going to \ndiscuss have been introduced before, by people often far more intelligent \nthan I am, but I want to give my opinion as someone who has operated ‘at \nthe coalface’ within many industry sectors. It’s as much a cathartic exercise \nas a knowledge share, but I hope I serve both purposes.\nWhen I was thinking of sub-titles for the book, I had initially decided to \ngo with ‘A handbook for security professionals’. This isn’t to say that I am \nan oracle on all things cybersecurity, more reference to the fact that I would \nhave liked to read such a book when I was starting out. I meet so many  \nsecurity leaders who have come from a non-technical background; many of \nPrefacexi\nthe topics within this publication come from discussions I have had with \nCISOs and security directors.\nIf you know me, you’ll know I am passionate about my work. I wrote on \nmy LinkedIn profile that I’d do (most aspects of) my job if I wasn’t paid to \ndo so. Unfortunately, passion can, occasionally, bring stubbornness and con-\nfirmation bias and that is why feedback is so important.\nI hope that my musings are useful. If you are an established security \nleader, perhaps I can offer a fresh perspective. If you’re new to our industry, \nI hope I have provided enough context and coverage of the important areas \nof our beloved profession. I hope that I have written this content to appeal \nto anyone who wishes to establish a more thorough understanding of why \ncybersecurity is more than a ‘nice to have’ for the modern business. \nTechnology is pervasive in all aspects of our daily lives. Personally, I think it \nis disrespectful to suggest that business leaders ‘don’t get all this cyber stuff’. \nIt feels a little patronising. Board members, the c-suite and senior managers \ndeal with risk every day – why should cyber risk be any different? These \neducated people have worked their way to the top of their companies. I as-\nsert that cybersecurity is misunderstood because we, the security function, \ndo a pretty rotten job of contextualizing information – I’ll cover some of this \nwithin the book.\n‘Cybersecurity is a mainstream business discussion…’ said many a news \narticle – why then are most cybersecurity books written in such a way as to \nrequire a degree in cybersecurity just to understand the concepts? Other \nbooks introduce aspects of the cybersecurity risk equation, but very few \nbring details covering threats, vulnerabilities and business impact. I have \naimed for this publication to cover each component of the cybersecurity risk \nequation, but in doing so I have had to remain pretty light on detail – inch \nthick, mile wide. I think that this was a solid trade-off.\nThis book follows a sequential structure. I would strongly suggest follow-\ning each chapter in turn. Part One provides the ‘why now’ of cybersecurity \n– why does our digitally enabled world require cyber risk to be treated via \nthe same processes as other business risk? Part Two details how to establish \na cybersecurity programme, and Part Three covers the constituent elements \nof a risk equation. Part Four is the book’s conclusion where I bring together \nthe concepts we have learnt. I wrap up each chapter with a ‘risk manage-\nment’ questions section – apart from Chapter 10, our conclusion. As you’ll \ngather when we move through chapters, I am not confident that ‘cybersecu-\nrity risk’ is really a discipline – it’s risk management with a cyber context. \nPlease take the time to answer the questions at the end of each chapter while \nthinking about your own organization – I believe it will add immeasurable \nPrefacexii\nvalue to your appreciation of cybersecurity and risk management maturity \nlevels in your business.\nFor the second edition, I’ve tried to clearly signpost fresh content in boxes \nacross each chapter. I have left the interviews untouched from the first edi-\ntion as the content is as thought-provoking and relevant as it was in 2018.\nI hope you enjoy the read – drop me a line over socials and let me know:\nTwitter/X: @stods21\nLinkedIn: https://www.linkedin.com/in/christopherjhodson/ \nACKNOWLEDGEMENTS\nThe ‘thank yous’ to the first edition felt a little like an Oscars acceptance \nspeech. Sorry about that. Candidly, I didn’t know if I would get the oppor -\ntunity to do this again. I will keep things brief this time.\nIf I speak with you in the office, on Twitter/X, LinkedIn, at conferences, \nboard meetings or in the pub, you have helped shape, challenge and culti-\nvate my perspectives on cybersecurity. Alex – I could not do any of my work without your love and \npatience. Thank you.\nTHIS PAGE IS INTENTIONALLY LEFT BLANKxiv\nPART ONE\nContextualizing \ncybersecurity risk\nTHIS PAGE IS INTENTIONALLY LEFT BLANK2\nWhy now? The \nonly constant \nis change01\nLEARNING OUTCOMES\nWhen I wrote this introduction in 2018, I aimed to illustrate the societal \nmindset shifts regarding cybersecurity. I outlined the impact on our industry \nof mainstream attention brought about by high-profile data breaches and an \nevolving threat landscape.\nWhat was the work of Hollywood blockbusters of yesteryear is playing \nout in front of our eyes. The consumer changes alone are startling: some \ncountries already have self-driving cars in operation. We have refrigerators \nthat tell you when food is out of date, smart homes that are orchestrated \nby devices you speak to and generative artificial intelligence algorithms \nworking on our behalf. Data are being used in ways unimaginable 10 years \nago. All of these advancements in technology bring with them enormous \nbenefits to the consumer, but they have a material impact on the controls \nand safeguards available to keep us safe from malicious and accidental \nthreats.\nIn 2023, the audience of this book will (hopefully) be as broad as it was in \n2018, with CIOs/CTOs and CISOs being the primary consumers. The current \nglobal macroeconomic volatility and continuing regulatory tightening have \nbrought cybersecurity to a wider executive audience and if you are a Chief \nFinancial Officer (CFO) or Chief Legal Officer (CLO) then I hope you find \nthe content useful, too – I have found both roles to be some of my closest \nstakeholders in recent years.\nOne constant from edition one is, well, the pace of technology change. \nWe have seen self-driving cars turn from concept to the periphery of \nthe mainstream. White goods still talk to themselves, but now Web3, \nContextualizing cybersecurity risk4\nthe metaverse and artificial intelligence are emerging as significant \ntechnological developments with profound implications for cybersecurity.\nIn 2018, enterprises were at the inception of transforming tightly coupled \napplication stacks into componentized software solutions delivered through \nmicroservices. The rationale was that these monoliths were not delivering \nwhat the business wants (or needs to survive). The adoption of microservices \nis something we will return to in coming chapters. The ubiquity of containers \nhas brought with it a need for technology orchestration and a revised \napproach to patch and vulnerability management. Companies are still – as \nthey were in 2018 – ‘failing fast’ and cloud applications and infrastructure \ncontinue to support exponential evolution in software development. It is not \njust technology that is changing; processes are being radically redesigned. \nAgile, lean methodologies are synonymous with success and bolting on \nsecurity as an afterthought isn’t an option.\nFor a book on cybersecurity, this chapter is light on security content, \nalthough please stay with me. One of the reasons enterprises are managing \nso much cyber risk is that they’re trying to apply security controls that were \nappropriate for a time when our technology landscape was seismically \ndifferent. To implement security controls commensurate with the existing \nthreat landscape, we must better understand the technologies widely \ndeployed in all companies.\nIf you’re a digital strategist, futurist, or editor of Wired, you might want \nto skip to Chapter 2. If you are interested in learning more about the driving \nforces behind this snowballing of cyber importance, read on.\nIn this chapter we will:\n• Answer the question, ‘what is digital transformation?’\n• Establish the characteristics of modern society which demand new \nways of approaching business problems.\n• Understand how to frame cybersecurity engagement for a wider \nboardroom discussion.\n• Answer the question, ‘what are Web3 and the Metaverse?’\n• Introduce Large Language Models and Generative AI.\n• Understand why you need to care about cybersecurity regardless of \ncompany size or sector.\nWhy now? The only constant is change5\nDigital transformation and the relevance of \na digital strategy\nIT’S A PHONE, JUST NOT AS WE KNOW IT.\nIn the summer of 2017, I bought a Blackberry KeyONE mobile phone. The \nrationale was simple: I liked the physical keyboard, Blackberry had shifted \nto Android and promised a monthly patch cycle. Aesthetically, it is a polished \nhandset. In my pride, I turned to my then four-year-old daughter to vindicate \nmy decision to impulse purchase. I‘d have taken a nod of appreciation, but \nthe confused look got me curious: ‘Dad, what’s that at the bottom of your \nphone?’\nDid the KeyOne come with one of those new-fangled headphone jacks? \nHad I missed a proprietary charging mechanism? I started to worry that \nI would be either returning the phone or shelling out cash for a fancy \nadapter (as a MacBook user, I‘m used to this). Her question was related to \nsomething far more fundamental and in no way facetious: she‘d not seen a \nkeyboard on a phone before. In fact, she’d only ever seen physical buttons \non her Peppa Pig toy handset.\nMy incredulity soon passed, and it dawned on me how quickly our world \nis changing, but it took a toddler’s curiosity for me to realize that the pace \nof change in our lives is frightening. My job presents me with examples of \ntechnological enhancement on a daily basis, but none more visceral and \nimpactful than a toddler confused by something ‘generation x’ intrinsically \nassociated with computers.\nCompanies are undergoing or have been through a digital transformation. \nAn information-centric world that requires a seismic shift in the way pro-\njects are delivered, businesses are run and risks are managed.\nWe are in a time where not only is technology rapidly changing, but also \nthe metrics and measurements that we use to gauge a company’s success. In \nyears gone by, profitability was measured through repeatable linear revenue \ngrowth, trustworthiness and reputation through the length of time a com-\npany had been in existence. ‘Brand power’ might not have been a phrase \nback then, but if it were, it would have been measured by these attributes.\nThe world I am describing used number of assets and physical locations \nto measure size and reach; a capital expenditure-oriented playbook that \nContextualizing cybersecurity risk6\nfeels archaic, even more so post-pandemic world. This world is less than  \n15 years old. How, then, have we moved so far in such a short space of  \ntime? Because we are in a world of exponential growth, an unprecedented \ndemand for data and an entirely new technology stack. These companies are \nnot successful just because they ‘went digital’; digitization was a means to an \nend. A method of achieving their business objectives.\nAt the cornerstone of digital transformation is a company’s digital strat-\negy. A digital strategy optimizes your IT spending as it tries to continuously \nimprove your IT systems (Rauser, 2016). A digital strategy is not merely \nshoehorning the latest and greatest technology into your environment \nbecause others are. A digital strategy is a thorough evaluation of all of your \ndigital assets and the creation of a plan to continuously improve them so \nthat they can positively contribute to your business objectives (Rauser, \n2016). A digital transformation should be driven by a business requirement \nto enhance an aspect of the company’s being: lower operational costs, \nincreased e-commerce revenue or improved customer satisfaction.\nFigure 1.1  Digital transformation\nFlexibility\nInno vation\nLower costs\nCustomer satisfaction\nCloud\nDelivered through:Derived beneﬁ ts\nDigital transformation\nMobility\nInternet of Things\nDevOps\nTechnology is a facilitator of digital transformation; it is not digital trans-\nformation per se. It is, however, an immutable fact that businesses can only \ndigitally enable themselves by embracing modern technology. There are \nmany exponential factors that contribute to the success of an organization \nundergoing a digital transformation:\nAn Exponential Organization (ExO) is one whose impact (or output) is dispro-\nportionally large – at least 10x larger – compared to its peers because of the use \nof new organizational techniques that leverage accelerating technologies (Ismail, \n2014).\nWhy now? The only constant is change7\nRecord-breaking technology adoption\nOn 6 October 2010, Kevin Systrom and Mike Krieger launched a free \nmobile application that would change the face of business innovation. Their \napplication was Instagram, and it played a fundamental role in the digitiza-\ntion and disruption associated with the photography industry over the past \n15 years. Systrom provided the first post to the service (in stealth mode at \nthe time), a photo of a dog, taken at a taco stand (Instagram, 2010).\nWas Systrom’s post a prescient statement around contemporary culture \nand the societal impacts of behaviours on the web? After all, no one knows \nif you’re a dog on the internet. Instagram was one of the first ‘kids in their \ngarages’ stories (I believe a metaphorical garage in this case) where within \nthree years, a company can go from inception to billion-dollar valuation. \nThe application was as conceptually simple as it was ingenious and relevant; \nit let users upload photos (and later on videos) to an online repository and \nlet friends comment, like and discuss the content. Instagram was the first \nservice to allow users to apply digital filters. Further social interaction was \nprovided through the inclusion of the now ubiquitous ‘hashtag’ feature \nalong with geolocation. The company did not rest on their laurels. Instagram \ncontinued to evolve and stay relevant. Instagram disrupted Instagram. Pretty \ncool.\nInstagram is frequently used as evidence of exponential growth, and my \ninclusion of its story is not one of author indolence but a testament to the \nimpact that this business had on society. Instagram took an elementary con-\ncept, the storage and dissemination of digital photography, and provided a \nset of social constructs that generated its sub-culture, an immersive, person-\nable experience. Postcards from holidays or processing physical films in the \npharmacy incurred time and cost delays. Instagram provided instantane-\nously dematerialized services.\nThe growth of Instagram fits our modus operandi of the exponential \ncompany: they had a million users in two months and 10 million in the first \nyear. By the end of 2017, Instagram had 800 million people using the plat-\nform in a month (CNBC, 2017). By way of perspective, that is almost triple \nthe population of the United States of America (Worldometers, 2018).\nWriting the second edition of this book in 2023, I cannot mention  \nexponential consumer technology adoption and not reference two record- \nbreaking applications taking the world by storm: TikTok and ChatGPT. \nBoth disruptive and with some profoundly important cybersecurity  \nconsiderations.\nContextualizing cybersecurity risk8\nChatGPT\nChatGPT reached 100 million active users after only two months of  \nbeing publicly available. Wow. Prior to ChatGPT’s explosion onto the  \nscene, TikTok (discussed momentarily) held the record for user adoption: \n100 million users in nine months (Daily Investor, 2023). Both applications \nexperienced the exponential growth commonly attributed to disruptive \ntechnologies.\nSo, what is ChatGPT and why the record-breaking adoption? ChatGPT, \ndeveloped by OpenAI, is a natural language processing AI model. I like to \nthink of ChatGPT as a search engine with a personality. As I write this \nupdate in June 2023, ChatGPT is based on the GPT-4 architecture, and has \ngained a reputation for generating human-like text, enabling it to perform \nvarious tasks such as answering questions, drafting content and engaging in \ncontextual conversations (OpenAI, 2021). Up until now, asking questions \nonline was largely limited to search engine queries for specific pieces of  \npersonal or professional information: the temperature in San Jose or ‘who  \nis Keyser Soze?’ ChatGPT, and the wave of generative AI solutions it has \nhelped bring to prominence, allow the user to ask for opinions, to review \ntheir work, to create a PowerPoint draft or prescribe medication to a patient. \nChatGPT is bringing automation to the masses, but at what cost?\nChatGPT isn’t sentient; it provides answers by leveraging a ‘large lan-\nguage model’ (LLM). In simple terms, this is a treasure trove of information \ntrawled from all over the internet. It may appear the system is thinking  \nfor itself, but in reality it will concatenate various pieces of data it feels will \nbest answer your question(s) and return the information in the shortest time \npossible.\nThere are myriad risks to using an LLM-based system as a un')
```

AI\nChapter 1  \nEnd-to-end data science workflows  \nwith Google Cloud\nChapter 2  \nGetting started with data science\nChapter 3  \nA practical guide for data science use cases\n03\n05\n14\n16\n3\nIntroduction\nData science is undergoing its most profound \ntransformation yet, as AI unlocks a wave of new \nuse cases that data scientists can solve faster and \nmore efficiently. With the help of AI, data science \nteams are now able to automate routine tasks, \nuse previously untapped unstructured data, and \nachieve new levels of efficiency. This guide helps \nyou get started with data science workflows on \nGoogle Cloud and offers a range of real-world \nuse cases (with code) for you to explore.\n\n4\nThe agentic shift:  \nReducing friction  \nand enabling flow \nThe data science workflow is inherently iterative  \nand often complex, forcing practitioners to constantly \nswitch contexts between creative exploration and \ntime-consuming, repetitive tasks. This mental burden \nof moving from high-level problem formulation to \nthe granular work of data cleaning, model evaluation, \nand optimization loops creates friction and breaks \nanalytical flow. AI agents and assistive AI tools help \nby automating these tedious steps, allowing you to \nfocus your expertise on what matters most: analysis, \ninterpretation, and solving the core business problem.Generative AI: Blending \nreal-world knowledge  \nwith your enterprise data\nAI enriches your analysis by introducing vast real-world \nknowledge. This is made possible through foundation \nmodels, which are pre-trained on a large corpus of \ngeneral information. These models bring an understanding \nof concepts and context that augments and enhances \nyour internal data. By blending this external intelligence \nwith your enterprise data, you can uncover deeper \ninsights, and formulate questions that require reasoning. \nFor instance, AI enables you to identify products with  \nhigh satisfaction “based on quality”—a subjective concept \nthe model can interpret without the need for manually \ndefined rules. This capability allows for more sophisticated \nand nuanced data analysis.\nData scientists can now take on new use cases with the help of AI\nTime series forecasting AI-powered agents and applications\nPredictive modeling Semantic search and RAG\nContribution analysis Automated content generation\nStatistical simulation Unstructured data analysis\nA/B testing Synthesis of real-world knowledge\nMultimodal data:  \nUnlocking the vast  \nmajority of your data\nFor a long time, valuable insights were locked in \nunstructured data like images, audio, documents,  \nand text. AI acts as a way to “see” inside images  \nand “understand” the nuances of language at scale.  \nThis breakthrough moves us beyond rows and  \ncolumns, enabling richer questions that combine  \ndata types in a single analysis. For example, you can  \nnow programmatically score review sentiment or \nanalyze call transcripts to find emerging issues.\n4\n5\nEnd-to-end \ndata science \nworkflows with \nGoogle CloudChapter 1\nAs a data scientist, your goal is to get from \na business question to a robust, actionable \ninsight as efficiently as possible. This section \nprovides a practical walkthrough of the \nintegrated Google Cloud tools to support \nthis modern workflow. It will demonstrate \nhow to move from setup to data access, \nmodel building, and production, all within  \na unified ecosystem built to keep you in  \nyour analytical flow.\n6\nYour central workspace: \nThe AI-first, multilingual \nnotebook \nNotebooks are the preferred development environment for \ndata science projects. But a fragmented environment forces \nconstant tool-switching, which disrupts flow. A typical data \nscience workflow might involve writing SQL in one tool for \nexploratory analysis or data transformation, exporting results, \nloading that into a local Python notebook, and then using  \na different tool for visualization. \nGoogle Cloud offers a flexible suite of notebook solutions \ntailored for enterprise data scientists, accommodating  \nvarious workflows and governance requirements. For a  \nfully managed and collaborative experience, Colab Enterprise \nnotebooks provide a secure, familiar interface that is deeply \nintegrated and directly accessible within BigQuery and  \nVertex AI. \nFor greater customization, Vertex AI Workbench delivers \npowerful, managed JupyterLab instances that serve as a \ndirect entry point to Serverless for Apache Spark, BigQuery, \nand Vertex AI. Teams preferring a fully Integrated Development \nEnvironment (IDE) experience can use Cloud Workstations \nwith the Cloud Code extension to provision secure, on-\ndemand Visual Studio Code environments. Additionally, \nGoogle Cloud supports self-hosted setups, by providing \ntools like the BigQuery JupyterLab and Visual Studio Code \nextensions, which enable seamless data exploration and \nmachine learning directly from a user's preferred IDE.\nSeamlessly blend languages\nThis is where the friction of context-switching disappears. \nIn this polyglot environment, you can query data using \nSQL in one cell and have the output instantly available as \na Python DataFrame in the next. You can also use Spark \nfor large-scale transformations without ever leaving the \nnotebook interface. This eliminates the mental friction of \nswitching between the declarative logic of SQL and the \nimperative thinking of Python.An AI-assisted workflow\nColab notebooks are designed with an integrated Data \nScience Agent to act as an intelligent partner. You can start \nwith a high-level goal in plain English, and the agent can \ngenerate a multi-step plan and the corresponding code. \nYou remain in control, reviewing and editing each step.\n6\nIn this section, we'll focus on Colab Enterprise—the preferred notebook experience for BigQuery users.\n7\nA practical example\nYour prompt\n“Train a model to predict ‘income_bracket’ from table: \ncensus_adult_income dataset.”\nThe agent then generates the SQL and Python code for each step, \nwhich you can execute, modify, and build upon. AI-powered code \ncompletion, error explanation and correction, and visualization \nfurther simplify the experience.\nColab notebooks offer an AI-powered unified development \nenvironment for analysis and rapid experimentationAI-generated plan\nLoad the census_adult_income table from BigQuery\nClean the data by removing rows with null values\nTrain a logistic regression model using BigQuery ML  \nto predict  income_bracket\nEvaluate the model’s accuracy1\n2\n3\n4\n7\n\n8\nAccessing and preparing \ndata without silos\nYour environment is set up—now you need data. This is \nwhere friction often arises, from handling different data \ntypes to rewriting code when datasets become too large \nfor a single machine.\nA unified, multimodal data foundation\nStructured data is typically only a small part of your data \nestate. Insights are locked in images, documents, and \naudio files. BigQuery allows you to manage all this data \nin one place. Using Object Tables and the ObjectRef data \ntype, you can treat unstructured data in Google Cloud \nStorage as if it were a standard SQL table. This means  \nyou can use the power and familiarity of SQL to analyze  \nimages or process documents directly. For example,  \nyou can call an AI function to extract text from a PDF  \nor analyze an image within your SQL query. All data, \nwhether structured or unstructured, is secured under a \nsingle, unified governance model, simplifying compliance.AI-assisted data preparation \nData scientists spend a lot of time on manual, often \nrepetitive data cleaning and transformation tasks. \nBigQuery accelerates this process with AI-assisted \ndata preparation. The built-in Gemini-powered editor \ntransforms data preparation from a manual coding \nexercise into an interactive, AI-guided workflow, allowing \nyou to focus on the logic of your transformations rather \nthan the syntax. As you work with your data, Gemini \nprovides context-aware suggestions for tasks like  \napplying transformations, standardizing data, and \nautomating schema mapping. \nThe new Data Engineering Agent in BigQuery goes  \nbeyond assistive AI and provides autonomous agentic \ncapabilities. The data engineering agent takes instructions \nin plain English, e.g., “Create a pipeline to load data from \nthe ‘ customer_orders’ bucket, standardize the date \nformats, remove duplicate entries based on order ID,  \nand load it into a BigQuery table named ‘ clean_orders’.” \nThen generates the required SQL code, builds the pipeline, \nand even creates basic unit tests. The agent can handle a \nwide range of data transformations and data engineering \ntasks, including ingestion, data quality, incorporating \ncustom business logic in pipelines, and data modeling.\nWant to learn more \nabout working with \nmultimodal data in \nBigQuery?\nExplore this notebook   \nwith code sample.\n\n9\nData enrichment and  \nGoogle third-party datasets\nUse the integrated data marketplace to discover  \nand use over 3,500 commercial and public datasets, \nwhich can be joined with your own tables without  \nany data movement or ETL processes. Leverage \nunique public datasets from Google, including \nthe Earth Engine Data Catalog for planetary-scale \nenvironmental data, Google Places Insights for \nlocation-based intelligence, and Google Trends  \nto analyze search interest over time. Blending your \nproprietary business data with global-scale external \nintelligence lets you dig deeper for insights.\nFlexible data processing  \nwith multiple engines\nBigQuery’s architecture allows you to bring your \npreferred processing engine—whether it's BigQuery's \nSQL engine or an open-source framework like \nServerless for Apache Spark—directly to a single, \nunified copy of your data. This avoids the need to \nmaintain separate data copies for different systems.\nYou can run serverless Spark directly from your Colab \nEnterprise or Jupyter notebook. This on-demand \nenvironment eliminates the need to provision and \nmanage clusters, offering fast startup and autoscaling. \nFor those who prefer Python-native libraries, BigQuery \nDataFrames (BigFrames) provide a pandas-like API that \ntranslates your Python code into optimized SQL for \nexecution on the BigQuery engine. This gives you the \nflexibility to use the right tool for the job—whether it's \nSQL, PySpark, or a pandas-style DataFrame—all while \nworking on the same underlying data.Data scientists can use Spark serverlessly or \na managed service via Dataproc\nKorbinian Schwinger\nTeam Lead Data Engineer, Public Value Technologies Public Value Technologies receives data feeds from our media partners \nfor our data mesh solution and AI applications. BigQuery data preparation \nallows our data analysts and scientists to rapidly integrate the data feeds \nthat standardize and preprocess the data in a low code way.”\nManaged Spark via \nDataprocServerless Spark via \nGoogle Cloud Serverless \nfor Apache Spark\nSpark clusters  \nas a serviceSpark jobs  \nas a service\nManaged Spark,  \nyour infrastructureManaged Spark,  \nmanaged infrastructure\nPay for cluster uptime\nFlexibility, control Ease of usePay for job runtime\n10\nA spectrum of model \ndevelopment options\nWith your data prepared, you can move on to the core \ntask of building and training a model. Google Cloud \nprovides a broad set of tools designed to meet you  \nwhere you are, whether you prefer the speed of SQL,  \nthe power of foundation models, or full control over \ncustom model development.\nBringing AI to your data with  \nBigQuery Machine Learning (BQML)\nA significant amount of time in any project is spent \nengineering pipelines to move data to a separate \nenvironment for model training. This creates friction, \nforcing teams to deal with disparate infrastructure \nand security policies and disrupting their flow. BQML \neliminates this by allowing you to train, evaluate, and \ndeploy machine learning models directly within the  \ndata warehouse using familiar SQL commands. This  \nis a practical approach for quickly building models like  \nlinear regression, k-means clustering, or time series \nforecasts without moving data. It also democratizes \nmodel building, enabling data analysts who are already \nSQL experts to develop powerful predictive models.\nTrain an ML model with SQL\nCREATE OR REPLACE MODEL `my_dataset.income_prediction_model`\nOPTIONS(model_type='LOGISTIC_REG') AS\nSELECT\n  * EXCEPT(income_bracket),\n  income_bracket as label\nFROM `bigquery-public-data.ml_datasets.census_adult_income`;\n\n11\nBQML offers a wide range of commonly used \nstatistical and predictive machine learning models \nthat are built into BigQuery for ease of use, \nincluding contribution analysis, linear regression, \nclassification, k-means clustering, matrix \nfactorization, time series forecasting, and more. \nThese built-in models greatly simplify the most \ncommon workflows for data scientists. As an \nadded benefit, you can perform a dry run before \ncreating the model to get an estimate of how \nmuch data the model will process to run.Leveraging pre-trained  \nfoundation models\nRather than building every model from scratch, you can \n“command” powerful, pre-trained foundation models.\nGen AI workflows\nBigQuery lets you use SQL functions that call on models \nlike the Gemini family to analyze or enrich your data. For \nexample, you can use the AI.GENERATE_TABLE function \nto extract structured information from unstructured text. \nUnder the hood, this is a managed remote function call  \nto a Gemini model endpoint, but to you, it's just another \nSQL function.\nSpecialized foundation models\nFor specific tasks, you can use state-of-the-art models. \nFor example, TimesFM, a model from Google Research \npre-trained on over 100 billion data points, is integrated \ninto BQML. This lets you generate highly accurate time \nseries forecasts with a simple SQL query, bypassing \nweeks of custom model development.With BigQuery ML, we’ve accelerated our model deployment to \nproduction 10x faster, enabling our sales teams to better serve \nour Pro customers with prioritized leads and deeper insights.” \nMani Pantangi\nSenior Manager, Data Science, The Home Depot\nWant to learn about \nusing AI functions  \nin BigQuery?\nThis notebook has code sampl es \nto help you get started.\n\n12\nQuick guide: When to use which tool?\nUse BigQuery ML when:\nYou want to rapidly build and \niterate on common model \ntypes (regression, classification, \nclustering, forecasting) directly \non your data in BigQuery using \nSQL. It's ideal for speed and for \nteams with strong SQL skills.Use Spark in BigQuery when:\nYou are familiar with Python and \nSpark, and are looking to use ML \nlibraries directly for distributed \ntraining and inference.Use Vertex AI when:\nYou need to build a custom  \nmodel with a specific \narchitecture, require advanced \nMLOps capabilities like \nexperiment tracking, or need \nto use Python frameworks like \nTensorFlow or PyTorch for deep \ncustomization and control.Training models using Spark in BigQuery\nIf you are familiar with Python and Spark and would like to use your \nfavorite machine learning libraries such as Spark ML, you can train, \nevaluate and run inferencing on your models directly in BigQuery \nwhile leveraging the distributed processing capabilities of Spark.\nCustom model development with Vertex AI\nFor projects that need more control, Vertex AI provides an end-to- \nend ML platform. Here, you can build, train, and tune custom models \nusing PyTorch, TensorFlow, or any other ML library. The power lies \nin the seamless integration. You can do feature engineering with \nBigQuery, train a custom model in Vertex AI, and then use that  \nmodel for inference directly from BigQuery via SQL.\nVector embeddings and vector search\nWhile not directly related to model training, a common data science \ntask is to find similar items within a large dataset. BigQuery lets you \ngenerate and use vector embeddings to perform vector search, \nenabling semantic understanding and similarity-based retrieval of \nmultimodal data.\nThe workflow to generate embeddings is straightforward: you create \na BigQuery ML remote model that references imported or Vertex AI \nembedding models. You then use that model with the ML.GENERATE_\nEMBEDDING function to create embeddings for multimodal data \nin BigQuery tables. You can create vector indexes using algorithms \nlike inverted file indexing (IVF) or the scalable approximate nearest \nneighbor (ScaNN) algorithm from DeepMind to accelerate similarity \nsearches. This allows you to build sophisticated semantic search, \nrecommendation, or segmentation systems without needing \nto manage a separate, specialized vector database. BigQuery \nis especially useful for these systems because it is all managed \nserverlessly, regardless of your batch or interactive needs.To solve inconsistent \nproduct matching, \nMercado Libre, the Latin \nAmerican ecommerce \nleader,  used BigQuery \nVector Search on \nproduct embeddings.  \nIt acted as a high-speed \nfilter, rapidly identifying \nan initial set of similar \nitems from their vast \ncatalog. This crucial first \nstep enabled other AI \nmodels to then perform \na more detailed analysis \non a pre-filtered list.\n12\n13\nFrom model to production  \nwith integrated MLOps\nA model only provides value when it's operationalized. The “last mile” MLOps challenge \nof moving a model from a notebook to a scalable, monitored production environment \nis addressed by a seamless integration between BigQuery and Vertex AI.\nCentralized feature management\nYou can use BigQuery to conveniently store \nfeatures for models built and used in BigQuery. \nFor centralized feature management across \nprojects in Google Cloud, you can use the \nVertex AI Feature Store to store, share, and \nreuse features. This prevents redundant data \nengineering work and, crucially, helps avoid \ntraining-serving skew by ensuring that the \nexact same feature logic is used for both model \ntraining and real-time inference. Features can  \nbe engineered in BigQuery and then ingested \ninto the Feature Store for consistent access.Automated model building  \nand management\nFor tabular data, Vertex AI AutoML automatically \nhandles feature engineering, model selection, \nand hyperparameter tuning to produce an \noptimized model. Any model you train, whether \nin BigQuery ML or Vertex AI, is automatically \nregistered in the Vertex AI Model Registry. From \nthere, with just a few clicks, you can version, \nevaluate, and deploy your models for batch or \nonline predictions, completing the end-to-end \nlifecycle from a single, integrated platform.\n13MLOps\nInterface\nAI/ML\nAnalytical \nengines\nUnified  \ngovernance\nMultimodal  \ndata foundationVertex AI Ops: Model registry, feature store, experiments, pipelines\nBigQuery ML  \nand AI Query \nEnginePySpark/Spark ML Vertex AIVector embeddings  \nand Search\nDataplex universal catalogIndustry-leading AI\nBigQueryIceberg Native \nCloud StorageSelf-managed \nopen formats \non GCSEasy access  \nto 3500+  \nGoogle and \npartner datasetsAI-first Colab notebooks + \ndata science agentThird-party notebooks and IDEs \nJupyter, Colab, VS Code\nBigQuery/ \nSQLDataproc/ \nServerless for \nApache SparkOSS and third-\nparty engines\nDeepMind\nGemini\nPartner and \nopen modelsGoogle Cloud's data and AI platform for data science and machine learning\nDataflow \nand Kafka\n14\nGetting started \nwith data scienceChapter 2\nHaving discussed the key Google Cloud \ncapabilities that streamline and simplify \ndata science workflows, this and the \nfollowing section will focus on their practical \napplication. We’ll first cover the necessary \nsetup to get hands-on and start exploring \ndata science use cases, and then look at a \nfew common examples that data scientists \nbuild in Google Cloud.\n\n15\nGoogle Cloud Account\nTo start using Google Cloud services, you first need \nto have an account. If you are new to Google Cloud, \nyou can sign up for a 90-day free trial that includes \n$300 in free Cloud Billing credits. You can use these \ncredits toward one or a combination of products. \nAdditionally, Google Cloud accounts also provide a \nfree tier to several Google Cloud products including \nBigQuery and Cloud Storage, allowing you limited \naccess at no cost. \nOnce you have your Google Cloud Account set up, \nyou will need to decide what notebook platform fits \nyour specific needs. As mentioned in Chapter 1, you \nhave several options to choose from. These include: \nBigQuery Studio, Vertex AI Workbenches, Cloud \nWorkstations, and self-hosted JupyterLab or  \nVisual Studio Code. \nHere’s how to get started with each of these options:\n15Self-hosted JupyterLab\nThe BigQuery Jupyter extension is a JupyterLab \nextension that brings powerful Google Cloud  \nfeatures directly into a user's local JupyterLab \nenvironment. It allows data scientists to treat  \nBigQuery or Spark on Dataproc as a seamless  \nbackend for their notebooks. Key features include  \nthe ability to run code on a BigQuery and Spark \n(serverless or clusters) without any local Spark \ninstallation, submit PySpark jobs directly from the \nnotebook, browse and edit objects on Cloud Storage, \nbrowse and preview BigQuery datasets, all from within \nthe familiar JupyterLab interface. This dramatically \nsimplifies the workflow for developing and deploying \ndata science pipelines on managed infrastructure.\nBigQuery Studio\nTo begin using BigQuery Studio:\nNavigate to the BigQuery page in the \nGoogle Cloud console.\nIf prompted, you'll need to enable any \nrequired APIs, such as the Dataform API,  \nto activate all of its features.\nOnce the interface loads, in the tab bar of \nthe editor panel, click the down arrow next \nto the plus sign, and from the floating menu, \nchoose Notebook >> Empty Notebook.\nIf it's your first time creating a code asset, \nyou may be prompted to choose a default \nstorage region.\nA new tab will then open, presenting you  \nwith a blank, untitled Colab notebook, ready \nfor you to start writing Python and SQL code.\nYou also have the option of starting with  \na template or uploading a notebook from  \nyour computer. For more information,  \nplease visit the Create notebooks page  \nin our documentation.1\n2\n3Self-hosted Visual Studio Code\nThe Google Cloud Code extension transforms Visual \nStudio Code into a powerful, integrated development \nenvironment for data professionals on Google Cloud. \nIt allows for direct interaction with BigQuery, enabling \nusers to seamlessly browse datasets, inspect schemas, \nand preview table data without leaving the IDE. For \nSpark, the extension provides comprehensive Dataproc \nintegration, allowing you to manage the entire lifecycle \nof your clusters and jobs. You can execute notebooks \non Dataproc clusters and serverless, submit jobs to \nboth traditional Dataproc clusters and serverless, and \ncreate and manage reusable serverless Spark runtime \ntemplates to ensure your notebooks execute with \nconsistent Spark configurations.\nThe project that your notebooks will be \nexecuted in\nThe BigQuery region\nThe Dataproc region that the extension  \nwill execute Spark notebooks in1\n2\n3Need to install the BigQuery Jupyterlab plugin?\nFind instructions on the Use the BigQuery  \nJupyterLab plugin page in our documentation.\nReady to install and use?\nVisit the Install the Cloud Code for VS Code extension  \npage in our documentation.\nOnce you have installed the extension and logged into \nGoogle Cloud, you will need to configure three settings:4\n5\n6\n16\nA practical guide \nfor data science \nuse casesChapter 3\nNow that your data science environment is set \nup and ready to go, let’s look at some practical, \nscenario-based use cases you can explore using \nBigQuery and Vertex AI. Each use case starts with \na common business challenge and walks through \nhow to use Google Cloud tools to solve it. These \nuse cases explore different statistical and machine \nlearning workflows that data scientists often use.\n\n17\nModernizing retail \ndemand forecasting\nThe business challenge\nIn retail, accurate demand forecasting is critical \nfor managing inventory. Overestimating demand \nleads to high storage costs and wasted product, \nwhile underestimating resources results in \nstockouts and lost sales. The core challenge \nlies in generating forecasts that are not only \naccurate, but also timely and highly granular. \nA business rarely needs to predict only its total \nsales. To make effective operational decisions,  \nit must forecast demand for every single item  \nin each specific store or region. Traditionally,  \nthis has been a complex and resource-intensive \ntask. It required data science teams to build, \nmanage, and retrain thousands of individual \nmodels—often one for each unique product-\nlocation combination. This process can be  \nslow, computationally expensive, and difficult  \nto maintain, making it hard for businesses to \nreact quickly to changing market conditions.\nThis use case uses the public Iowa Liquor  \nSales dataset . It provides a practical example, \ncontaining daily sales records for various \nproducts across the state. While it focuses  \non liquor sales, the principles and techniques  \nare directly transferable to any retail scenario.  \nThe fundamental challenge is the same—\nwhether you are forecasting demand for  \na specific t-shirt size in a particular store,  \na smartphone model in a city, or a brand of \ncereal in a specific supermarket. The goal is  \nto find a scalable process that can handle  \nthis complexity efficiently, directly where  \nthe data resides.The data science approach\nThis use case addresses forecasting by \nimplementing and comparing two powerful  \ntime series models using BigQuery ML. First,  \nwe’ll use ARIMA, a well-established statistical \nmodel, which requires us to train on historical \ndata before generating a forecast. Then, we use \nBigQuery’s AI.FORECAST function, which uses \nTimesFM, a Google Research foundation model  \nto produce zero-shot forecasts without a  \nseparatetraining step. This approach eliminates  \nthe need to move massive datasets to a separate \nenvironment for training. By comparing these \nmethods, you can easily evaluate their tradeoffs  \nto determine which one best meets your needs.\nHigh-level flow\n• Prepare the data: First aggregate raw sales data \ninto two distinct tables—one for a single time series \nand another for a multiple, granular time series\n• Forecast a single time series: We walk through \nforecasting total sales over time using both the \nARIMA and TimesFM models\n• Visualize single series results: The forecasts from \nboth methods are plotted together to assess their \nperformance and differences\n• Forecast multiple time series: Scale the analysis \nto forecast sales for each county and item_name \ncombination, demonstrating how to handle \nthousands of series at once\n• Visualize multiple series results: Finally, visually \ncompare forecasts for a specific county-item  \npair to compare the model’s performance at  \na granular levelUse case 1 Get the notebook\n18\nUse case 2\nAssessing environmental risks to \nprotect agricultural investments\nThe business challenge\nFor agribusinesses and their investors and \ninsurers, accurately quantifying the financial  \nrisk of a production shortage is a major \nchallenge. The direct impact of events like a  \nfire or drought is a loss of crop yield, which  \nleads to revenue loss, supply chain disruptions, \nand financial consequences.\nAs global natural events become more frequent, \noperational costs are rising, driven by higher \nprices and reduced availability of insurance. \nCurrent insurance models often rely on broad, \nregional risk assessments rather than property-\nspecific data. To make informed decisions about \ncapital investment, such as where to buy new \nland or plant new crops, and to better protect \nexisting assets, stakeholders require a more \nprecise method for evaluating risk. \nThe business need is a data-driven model that \ncan assess and quantify this risk at a highly \ngranular level, for example, down to an individual \nparcel of land. Relying on historical data alone  \nis reactive—a forward-looking, dynamic view  \nof risk is essential to guide financial and \noperational strategy.The data science approach\nThis use case leverages the vast repository of \npublic satellite imagery and environmental data \navailable through Google Earth Engine, which \nis available as rasterized data directly within \nBigQuery. Analyzing key raster datasets identifies \nand monitors critical risk factors such as land \nsurface temperature, water availability, and \nvegetation density (fuel load). This allows for  \nthe creation of a dynamic wildfire risk intelligence \nsystem. By joining this environmental data with \nthe geographic locations of vineyards and other \nassets, stakeholders can generate precise risk \nassessments for specific areas of interest.\nHigh-level flow\n• Access Chilean regions of interest: Use Overture \nMaps in BigQuery Sharing to access regional data. \nCombine this data with official wine production \nstatistics to map regions of interest and their \ngeographical boundaries using the GEOGRAPHY \ndata type in BigQuery.\n• Explore Earth features: Quickly explore Earth \nfeatures using Earth Engine’s public datasets. \nAnalyze elevation, wildfires, water irrigation, and \navailability using datasets such as the NASA Digital \nElevation Model, GFSAD Cropland, and GLOBathy \nGlobal Lakes Bathymetry.\n• Perform raster analysis with ST_RegionStats:  \nApply the ST_RegionStats  function to compute \naggregate statistics (e.g., mean, sum, standard \ndeviation) for raster data within Chilean regions \ndefined by BigQuery geographies. This allows for \nsummarizing raster insights over vector boundaries.\n• Visualize and interpret results: Export or connect \nBigQuery results to geospatial visualization tools \n(e.g., GeoViz, Looker Studio, Looker, Carto) to \ninterpret and communicate findings effectively.\n• Get actionable insights: Develop a ML model to \npredict risk areas and take timely actions.\n18Get the noteb")

Associate Professor at Lead City \nUniversity Ibadan, Nigeria. Additionally, she serves as a Senior Research Associ -\nate within the Faculty of Engineering and Built Environment at the University of \nJohannesburg, South Africa. In 2020, she completed a post-doctoral research project focused on developing a rapid re-skilling framework for Construction 4.0 in Sub-Saharan Africa. This research was conducted at the Department of Construction Management and Quantity Surveying, University of Johannesburg, South Africa.\nDuring her post-doctoral tenure, Professor Adepoju contributed significantly to \nthe academic landscape, publishing multiple articles and a book on the topic of reskilling human resources for Construction 4.0, with implications for industry, aca -\ndemia, and government. Subsequently, she efficiently coordinated an entrepreneur -\nship skills program at Pan African University, University of Ibadan, during the period 2020–2021. Professor Adepoju’s expertise spans various areas, including re-skilling in the Fourth Industrial Revolution, Construction 4.0, Industry 4.0, human capac -\nity development, human capital in renewable energy, assessing skill gaps to meet the demands of Construction 4.0, evaluating innovative skills, and management of Water, Energy and Food nexus.\nProfessor Nnamdi Ikechi Nwulu is a Professor of Sustainable Cyber-Physical \nEngineering Systems and Director of the Centre for Cyber Physical Food, Energy\xa0& Water Systems at the University of Johannesburg. Additionally, he is the Chair -\nholder of the South Africa/Switzerland Bilateral Chair in Blockchain Technology. His research endeavors concentrate on the practical applications of digital technolo-gies, mathematical optimization techniques, and machine learning algorithms within food, energy, and water systems.\nProfessor Nwulu’s scholarly contributions encompass eight published books \nand over 70 indexed journal publications, earning him the prestigious 2021 NRF Research Excellence Award for Early Career/Emerging Researchers in the Engineer -\ning/Technology Category. He was also recognized as a finalist for the 2020/2021 TW Kambule-NSTF-South32 Award in the Emerging Researcher category and was hon -\nored with the 2020 UJ Vice Chancellor’s Most Promising Young Researcher award. His memberships include the South African Young Academy of Science and various other professional associations. Furthermore, Professor Nwulu is the editor-in-chief of the Journal of Digital Food Energy and Water Systems  and is Associate Editor for \nthe IET Renewable Power Generation  and African Journal of Science, Technology, \nInnovation\xa0& Development.\nDr. Love Opeyemi David  is an interdisciplinary social scientist, proficient in project \nmanagement, resource management, management science, digital transformation, \nand innovation management. He bagged first class honors in his bachelor of technol-ogy and earned a master of technology with distinction in project management tech -\nx About the Authors\nnology from the Federal University of Technology, Akure, Nigeria. His academic \njourney culminated in a Ph.D. degree from the University of Johannesburg, South Africa.\nDr. David has showcased his expertise by publishing scholarly articles in Scopus-\nindexed journals. His diverse research interests encompass project management, innovation management, management science, Water-Energy-Food Nexus project delivery, circular economy, sustainable development/sustainability, and cutting-edge technologies of the Fourth Industrial Revolution (including blockchain technology, Internet of Things, and artificial Intelligence). He is currently the managing editor of the Journal of Digital Food, Energy and Water Systems.\nPart I\nIntroduction and Background\n\nDOI: 10.1201/9781003522102-2  3\n1Introduction\n1.1 BACKGROUND TO THE STUDY\nThe breadth and depth of the Fourth Industrial Revolution have shown that all liv -\ning and non-living things will be affected by the technologies of the revolution. The \nFourth Industrial Revolution, also known as 4IR, brings a new dimension, opportu -\nnities, and predictable risks with high uncertainty of impact. Moreover, the extent and magnitude of its influence across the positive and negative dimensions are yet unknown, as there are endless combinations and tests of technologies of the Fourth Industrial Revolution used for different purposes. This level of uncertainty has caused academic fear and industrial tensions. Nevertheless, the Fourth Industrial Revolution and its technologies are an efficiency booster, a catalyst, a medium of technological discovery, and a ray of hope for an innovative breakthrough that will positively change the usual way of doing things in every aspect of life. Thus, the 4IR alters the pattern of getting things done and shifts the social, technological, and research equilibrium, necessitating a period of adjustment and the consequence of adjustment. Hence, there is a need for a framework to offer technological insights.\nThe Fourth Industrial Revolution combines economies of scale and econo -\nmies of scope in generating a fusion of a new array of technologies, which, to a great extent, digitalized production process, whereby the process is monitored by the Internet of Things (IoT), cloud computing, and computers (Dombrowski and Wagner 2014). Postelnicu and Calea (2019) opined that the era of the Fourth Industrial Revolution is an era of digitalization, whereby digital technologies are applied to various fields of manufacturing, medicine, spatial missions, and avia -\ntion, among others, without compromising quality, which will lead to production cost minimization and higher economic reform. In describing how revolutionary, strategic, and dramatic the Fourth Industrial Revolution is, Schwab (2016) stated that the world is currently facing a shift across all industries, evident in new busi -\nness models, changes in systems, and the reshaping of production mechanisms, consumption patterns, and transportation systems, whereby these changes are historical in terms of speed, size, and scope. The author further opined that the Fourth Industrial Revolution is a revolution for three reasons. The first reason is the velocity. Schwab (2016) opined that, unlike previous industrial revolutions, the Fourth Industrial Revolution is operating at an exponential rate rather than a linear pace because new technology is becoming more capable technology. The second reason is the breadth and depth of the revolution, which is not only chang -\ning the ‘what’ or ‘how’ of doing things but changing ‘who’ we are and redirecting our lifestyle and pattern. This has also led to a paradigm shift in business, indi -\nvidual lives, and the economy. The third reason is the impact of 4IR on systems, whereby an entire system is transformed across industries, companies, societies, and countries. Moreover, Deloitte Insights (2020) and David et\xa0al. (2022) posited \n4 Framework for Fourth Industrial Revolution Technologies\nthat the 4IR, also known as Industry 4.0, is the marriage between physical assets \nand advanced digital technologies such as artificial intelligence, drones, Internet of Things (IoT), robots, cloud computing, 3D printing, autonomous vehicle, and nanotechnology among others, which analyze, communicate, and act upon infor -\nmation that will enable consumer organizations and society to be more respon -\nsive and flexible to make more intelligent and data-driven decisions. The Fourth Industrial Revolution fuses the biological, physical, and digital world, combining new and emerging technologies and bringing humanity to globalization, whereby risks and opportunities are shaped (Mitrović 2020; Dogo et\xa0al. 2019).\nMoreover, from the perspective of health, Castro e melo and Araujo (2020) \nopined that the 4IR is changing the dimension and system of the health sector in terms of the mechanism of understanding the health sector, transformation in the system, method, practice of diagnosis and administration of treatment, manage-ment style of the health system, and alteration of the relationship between patients and health professionals. The United Nations Industrial Development (2019) opined that the Fourth Industrial Revolution heralded a period where innovation is fast-paced, combining complex technology, making innovation more multi-  \ndisciplinary, unplanned, collaborative, disruptive, and unpredictable. To justify that there is no universal definition of the 4IR, Soh and Connolly (2021) gave a unified description of the 4IR. The author opined that from the ontological point of view, the 4IR entails the interpretation of digital ecosystems with human lives and the environment. This also includes the broadened application of digitaliza -\ntion in economic activity, social dimensions, and political configuration. They argued that human bodies are becoming data, and there are no differences between the virtual world and reality due to digitalization. The second perspective of the 4IR description revealed that virtual and real-world interpenetrations will alter current and existing business models, whereby the supply chain is digitalized, trackable in real time, and automated, which will allow a reduction of waste, cre -\nation of new markets, and the tendency to lower prices of goods and services. The Department of Higher Education and Training (2020) opined that 4IR is changing in an exponential means the way humans create, distribute, and exchange value, which is resulting in a systemic change across different aspects of human life and sector with cross-cutting implications in social cycles, political dynamics, cultural mechanism, and economic pragmatism. The department further opined that the elements of the 4IR are divided into three along with their attributes, whereby this book divided it into four along with necessary modifications, as shown in Figure\xa01.1.\nThe Fourth Industrial Revolution has made remarkable impacts in several fields. \nMalomane et\xa0al. (2022) opined that through the application of the 4IR technolo-gies in the construction industry, incidents of site injuries, and accidents could be curtailed, whereby construction sites are monitored and controlled, and the workforce can be trained virtually on safety and handling of equipment, ensuring accident prevention and the technologies can aid in better reporting, enhanced vis -\nibility, better communication, and improved workflows. Moreover, in the review of the 4IR, Deloitte (2020) stated that through the technologies of 4IR, there \n5 Introduction\nwould be disruptions in different organizations, especially in business strategies; \nthe report noted that many organizations are utilizing big data analytics, artifi -\ncial intelligence (AI), Internet of Things (IoT), and cloud computing to generate data for improved decisions-making, which will drive up revenue. UNIDO (2019) categorized the benefits of applying 4IR into three dimensions of sustainability. The economic benefits include enhanced control over the production process, lower transaction costs leading to higher revenue, competitive edge as a result of improved productivity, customer feedback mechanism leading to improved prod -\nuct, enhanced product quality, efficient management of global value chain pro -\ncesses in real-time across a great distance, a shift from mass production to mass customization, creation of intelligent production systems, conversion of customer behavior in creating sellable products, digital blend of manufacturing and service activities in boosting economic growth, and enhancement of predictive main -\ntenance; 4IR acts as a catalyst to the United Nations Sustainable Development \nFIGURE 1.1  Classifications of 4IR (Department of Higher Education and Training 2020, \np.\xa022)\n6 Framework for Fourth Industrial Revolution Technologies\nGoals (SDG), to ensure leapfrogging in the advancement of some sectors of the \neconomy and the safety and security of transaction data. Moreover, environ -\nmental benefits include the reduction of pollutants and emissions of greenhouse gases, providing greater resource efficiency, enabling accessibility and stability of natural resources like water and electricity; prevention, reduction, and elimi-nation of waste; optimization of circular economy business model; capability to plan, analyze, predict, and control the performance of products; energy efficiency and energy savings; the capability of mitigating climate change; monitoring and tracking of environmental systems; and accelerating global climate agreements. Furthermore, the social benefits include enhancement of innovation and creativity, improvement in health, human cognition and physical capabilities, better working climate and workers’ productivity, creation of opportunities for vulnerable and disadvantaged population groups, and creation of an integration path for SMEs to participate in global markets and ensure the transfer of knowledge.\nSchwab (2015) also opined that the 4IR has the potential to improve quality \nof life, raise global income level, increase the ability of consumer access to the digital world, ensure a drop in the cost of communication and transportation, and enable the effectiveness of global supply chain and logistics. Goncharov (2020) analyzed the impact of the Fourth Industrial Revolution. Goncharov included automation of business, service, and production systems, quick penetration into the global market resulting from digitalization in research\xa0& development, sales, distribution and marketing, changing demands of goods and services, increased collaborative innovation, change in customer expectation and buying patterns, flexibility and sustainability of production assets, provisions of digital platforms in interaction and engagement between government and citizens, and restructur -\ning of human’s nature of privacy, consumer habits, accessibility, and changing of the dynamics to work and leisure. Gravier (2019) stated that the Fourth Industrial Revolution occurred in three stages and spheres, leading to several opportunities and risks. The first sphere is the digital-physical sphere, where real-world data, inputs, and innovations enter a digital sphere. The second stage is the digital sphere, where data and information in the digital sphere are processed into infor -\nmation, raising issues of security and integrity. The third sphere is the human sphere, which deals with the technical and digital skills needed in the integration process.\nThe Fourth Industrial Revolution is a digital enabler: redirecting, repurposing, \nand reconfiguring different sectors of the economy. For instance, it is changing strategic management decisions by influencing the decision-making process of senior executives and chief executive officers, who, before, had been subjected to long hours of brainstorming. It is also altering the administrative, business, and policy approach of tactical management, whereby middle-level managers rely on digital technologies occasioned by the Artificial Intelligence technology of 4IR in dissecting strategic goals and objectives. Also, the operation level of management utilizes the 4IR technologies in carrying out various tasks to meet organization outputs. In essence, it alters the organization’s culture, vision, mission, objectives, and competitive strategy in building strategic agility.\n7 Introduction\nMoreover, the educational system is not exempt from the Midas touch of the \nFourth Industrial Revolution. It has changed the mode of learning, the dynamics \nof teaching, the medium of accessing knowledge, and the period of disintegrat -\ning knowledge. Moreso, the 4IR in the educational syst')
```

...................................................................................................................... 177\n\nxiii\nPreface\nDuring this era of digital change, the possibilities that data analysis, ML and cloud com -\nputing present to industries are limitless. These technologies, when brought together, \nenable businesses to capture enormous amounts of data, gain insight into them and improve their business processes. This book is an attempt to address the issues surround -\ning all three areas, by providing a worthy source that integrates theory and practice.\nThe journey begins with insights into what data analytics are like, where it \nstarted, how it looks like in a big data era. The book then turns to relevant challenges that industries are currently facing in the data management sphere, analysing neces -\nsary tools and technologies such as Apache Hadoop, Tableau and Power BI, which are crucial for analytical processes. The emphasis on statistical methods and ML toolchains equips the audience with the necessary skill set to comprehend predictive analytics models and the deployment of such models.\nThe book discusses the potential of ML by introducing methods like neural net -\nworks, supervised and unsupervised learning, regression, clustering and others and demonstrating how they are used in many fields. Along with describing cloud com -\nputing’s architecture, advantages and integration with analytics to promote scalabil -\nity and agility, it also examines the revolutionary role of cloud computing. Examples from e-commerce, logistics, healthcare and education highlight the transformative potential of combining data analytics, ML and cloud computing, and driving inno -\nvation and efficiency across industries. These technologies empower businesses to make data-driven decisions, optimize operations and improve user experiences. The book also focuses on important aspects such as security and privacy concerns which are very important in the cloud age. Also, it provides a good exploration of new advancements that are expected to give a beacon of hope on the advancements of AI analytics in businesses and the society as a whole.\nThe unique highlights that distinguish this book and demonstrate its transforma -\ntive potential in the intersection of data analytics, ML and cloud computing are as follows:\n• It provides thorough insights into the potent fusion of cloud computing, ML \nand data analytics, fusing theoretical knowledge with real-world applications.\n• It highlights the enormous potential of these technologies by discussing \nreal-world use cases in a variety of industries, including e-commerce, healthcare, education and logistics.\n• It provides a thorough examination of state-of-the-art ML tools and algo -\nrithms, highlighting how they may optimize data analytics for better decision-making.\n• It discusses the most recent developments, difficulties and security issues in \nthe rapidly changing field of data analytics using cloud computing and ML.\n• It offers a forward-looking perspective by examining the revolutionary \neffects of AI, ML and big data technologies on several sectors, paying par -\nticular attention to new advancements.\nxiv Preface\nThis book is appropriate for students, researchers and professionals who would like \nto enhance their knowledge of these interrelated forms of technology. With its struc -\ntured approach and practical insights, it serves as both a learning tool and a refer -\nence for navigating the complexities of the data-driven world. This book is aimed at encouraging readers to become actively involved in the development of data analyt -\nics, ML and cloud computing.\nOne of the most significant areas that now affect decision-making across a range \nof industries, including government, business, healthcare and education, is data analytics. It entails examining unprocessed datasets to identify novel approaches to decision-making that will assist the company in resolving intricate issues using the information gathered. Chapter\xa01, “Data Analytics: An Overview”, explores the development of data analytics, from conventional methods to the fusion of data sci -\nence and big data. It focuses on how ML and AI-driven techniques have replaced descriptive and predictive analytics. Furthermore, this chapter examines how big data analytics has transformed several se')

On the one hand, new research, tools and technologies are currently being developed to harness the increasingly large quantities of data being generated within our society. On the other, big data curricula at universities are still based on the computer science knowledge systems established in the 1960s and 70s. The gap between the theories and applications is becoming larger, as a result of which current education programs cannot meet the industry’s demands for big data talents.\nThis series aims to refresh and complement the theory and knowledge framework \nfor data management and analytics, reflect the latest research and applications in big data, and highlight key computational tools and techniques currently in development. Its goal is to publish a broad range of textbooks, research monographs, and edited volumes that will:\n –Present a systematic and comprehensive knowledge structure for big data and data science research and education\n –Supply lectures on big data and data science education with timely and practical reference materials to be used in courses\n –Provide introductory and advanced instructional and reference material for stu -\ndents and professionals in computational science and big data\n –Familiarize researchers with the latest discoveries and resources they need to advance the field\n –Offer assistance to interdisciplinary researchers and practitioners seeking to learn more about big data\nThe scope of the series includes, but is not limited to, titles in the areas of \ndatabase management, data mining, data analytics, search engines, data integration, \nNLP, knowledge graphs, information retrieval, social networks, etc. Other relevant topics will also be considered.\nZhi\xa0Liu  • Y ao\xa0Xiao\nAI-Enabled Learning \nEngagement Analysis\nRevolutionizing Education with\xa0AI\nISSN 2522-0179      ISSN 2522-0187  (electronic)\nBig Data Management\nISBN 978-981-96-6893-9     ISBN 978-981-96-6894-6  (eBook)\nhttps://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-96-6894-6\n© The Editor(s) (if applicable) and The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapore \nPte Ltd. 2026 \nThis work is subject to copyright.
