For further details see license information in the chapter.\nvPreface\nIn the rapidly evolving landscape of education, the integration of artificial intelli -\ngence (AI) has become a transformative force, reshaping not only the way learning \nis delivered but also how student learning engagement is measured and enhanced. The potential for AI to revolutionize educational practices has been a central focus of research and development, particularly in understanding and improving learning engagement. This book draws from ongoing efforts to explore AI’s applications in educational contexts, particularly in enhancing student involvement through intel -\nligent systems and data-driven insights.\nThe primary aim of this work is to examine the intersection of AI technologies \nand learning engagement, offering a comprehensive analysis of how AI can be lev -\neraged to promote deeper cognitive, emotional, and behavioral engagement in edu -\ncational settings. By analyzing the current landscape of AI in education, this book seeks to build bridges between technological innovations, teaching practices, and student outcomes, fostering collaborations between academia, industry, and educa -\ntional practitioners.\nAt the core of this exploration is the recognition that learning engagement is not \nmerely a measure of time spent in class or the completion of assignments, but a multidimensional construct that encompasses emotional and cognitive aspects of student involvement. AI tools have the capacity to capture and analyze these dimen -\nsions, providing real-time data that can inform adaptive learning systems, personal -\nized feedback, and more effective teaching strategies. Thus, the integration of AI into educational systems holds the promise not only of improving academic perfor -\nmance but also of supporting the overall growth and well-being of students.\nAs we look toward the future of education, it is essential that AI’s application \nshould be driven by a deep understanding of educational needs and challenges. This book is structured to offer insights into the ways AI can be applied in real-world educational scenarios, ranging from classroom interactions to online learning envi -\nronments. Through case studies, research findings, and expert opinions, the chapters will explore how AI can address key challenges in education, including personal -\nized learning and the fostering of critical thinking skills.\nvi\nIn conclusion, this work aims to contribute to the ongoing discourse on AI’s role \nin education, providing readers with both a theoretical foundation and practical \ntools to understand and implement AI technologies for improving student engage-ment and educational outcomes.\nWuhan, China   Zhi\xa0Liu  Preface\nviiCompleting this book would not have been possible without the support of many \nindividuals. Liu, Y . Xiao, AI-Enabled Learning Engagement Analysis , Big Data \nManagement, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-96-6894-6_1Chapter 1\nOverview of\xa0Learning Engagement \nResearch\n1.1  What is Learning Engagement\nWhat students do during college counts more in terms of desired outcomes than who they \nare or even where they go to college\n—George D.\xa0Kuh\nWhat is the key to driving students to success? Is it the student’s talents or the learn -\ning environment? Professor Kuh from Indiana University believes that the key to \ninfluencing the quality of learning is the time and energy learners engage in learning \nactivities [ 1]. The term learning engagement was first proposed in the 1980s [ 2]. \nHowever, looking back at the research on learning engagement, we will find that \nthis is a gradually enriching process.\nIn the 1930s, Tyler first proposed the concept of “time on task,” highlighting that \nthe more time students invest in learning, the more they are likely to achieve [ 3]. \nMuch of the research on learning engagement is based on learning models and stud -\nies on the relationship between time and achievement. One of the first and most \nimportant of such models is John Carroll’s model in school learning [ 4]. Carroll \nbelieved that the factors influencing learning outcomes include aptitude (the time \nstudents need to learn), opportunity to learn (the time allocated for learning), perse -\nverance (the time students are willing to invest), quality of instruction, and ability to \nunderstand instruction. And he noted that it has always been a matter of some aston -\nishment to me that I am credited with directing attention to time in learning, an \nexceedingly obvious variable that must have been in the minds of educators over the \ncenturies and that has figured heavily in work of theorist and experimenter on learn -\ning [ 5]. Influenced by Carroll’s research, many researchers not only focus on the \ndimension of time but also propose new insights into the composition of learning \ninput from a more detailed perspective.\nIn 1982, Professor Pace from the University of California, Los Angeles, pro -\nposed the concept of quality of effort, arguing that it is not enough to just focus on \n2\nthe time students put into learning, but also to pay attention to the degree of stu -\ndents’ concentration in the learning process, that is, to pay attention to both the \nquality and quantity of learning engagement [ 6]. At the same time, some researchers \nwere influenced by educational psychology and believed that it was not enough to just focus on the time invested in learning behaviors or classroom participation. There should be a deeper engagement behind learning engagement [ 7]. Adhering to \nthis view, Newmann from the University of Wisconsin–Madison proposed for the first time that learning engagement should also include affective engagement through his years of classroom observation and case studies of students [ 8]. This \ntheory further defines the different types of engagement included in learning engage -\nment and also opens up new avenues for studying psychological engagement in learning. Subsequently, the emotional and behavioral engagement model proposed by Professor Wentzel from the University of Maryland added attention to students’ emotional engagement while only focusing on learning behavior. The emotion here is different from Newmann’s view that students have a sense of value after gaining knowledge. Instead, it focuses on students’ emotional experiences in learning, such as feeling excited and happy or feeling worried and angry [9].\nIn subsequent research, Professor Connell from Portland State University, based \non the theory of psychological dichotomy, divided the emotional engagement in learning engagement into emotional engagement and cognitive engagement for the first time, forming the prototype of a three-dimensional framework of learning engagement [10]. Based on this, Professor Fredericks from Union College proposed a three-dimensional framework for learning engagement, which is defined as behav -\nioral engagement, emotional engagement, and cognitive engagement [ 11]. In subse-\nquent research, as collaborative learning patterns became more prevalent in the classroom, Fredericks added social engagement to the original three-dimensional framework [12]. It can be seen that there is no unified understanding of the compo -\nsition of learning engagement. However, in much of the research on learning engagement, most scholars endorse the three dimensions or subtypes of learning engagement proposed by Fredricks in their seminal review of the literature: emotion (affective), cognition (cognitive), and behavior (behavioral).\n1.2  Relating Predicti ve Studies to\xa0Learning Engagement\nTo this day, research on learning engagement is still a hot topic in educational psy -\nchology, in particular linking students’ involvement and participation in academic tasks and activities to their achievement and long-term outcomes. In addition, learn -\ning engagement has been central to addressing both high school and college non-completion.\nIn the earliest discussions of learning engagement and dropout in the K-12 litera -\nture, the emphasis was mainly on disengagement as a key factor contributing to dropout, with engagement often defined in contrast as its direct opposite (e.g., lack of participation in vs. participation in school). As interest in dropout grew, studies 1 Overview of\xa0Learning Engagement Research\n3\nidentified that learning engagement was predictive of dropout or completion. For \nexample, Professor Finn from the University at Buffalo, State University of New\xa0York, proposed the participation-identification model to investigate the impact of factors such as student attendance and homework completion on academic achievement, school adaptation, and dropout behavior, underscoring the important impact of learning behavior on academic achievement and dropout behavior [13]. Similarly, Wehlage proposed a dropout prevention model focused on the school’s role in addressing learning engagement in terms of educational engagement and school membership, which seem to align with behavioral and affective engagement, respectively [14].\nIn addition to research on preventing student dropouts, learning engagement is \nalso considered an important variable in predicting students’ completion of learning outcomes and course grades. Christenson et\xa0al. argued for shifting focus from the prediction of a negative outcome, dropout, to the promotion of school completion with competence [15]. The authors underscored the importance of a systemic approach, linking with schools, families, and community resources to provide per -\nsonalized interventions in support of school completion. Furthermore, with the application of self-regulation theory in learning, research by Zimmerman B.\xa0J. pointed out that there is a significant relationship between self-regulated learn -\ning and students’ academic performance [16].\n1.3  The Role of\xa0Learning Engagement \nin\xa0Educational Reform\nTo advance educational reform and enhance students’ engagement in academic pur -\nsuits, numerous countries have undertaken extensive research projects focusing on educational transformation, with learning engagement being at the core of such reforms. In 2000, a research team from Indiana State University launched and pro -\nmoted the National Survey of Student Engagement (NSSE) initiative. By 2006, nearly 1100 higher education institutions, encompassing a total of 1.16 million stu -\ndents, participated in this nationwide survey, establishing it as one of the largest academic learning engagement studies in the United States [17]. The findings revealed a significant correlation between students’ learning experiences, academic outcomes, and academic growth with their level of learning engagement. The study also illuminated the profound impact of learning engagement on multiple factors such as student retention rates, graduation rates, faculty interaction, high-impact educational practices, campus resource utilization, critical thinking, overall satis-faction, community interaction, diversity and inclusivity, self-efficacy, and aca -\ndemic motivation. Through a rigorous and comprehensive assessment framework, the NSSE provided invaluable empirical evidence to higher education institutions, effectively contributing to the enhancement of teaching quality and the deepening of educational reform. Furthermore, the NSSE has had a significant impact globally 1.3 The Role of\xa0Learning Engagement in\xa0Educational Reform\n4\nand is now regarded as one of the most important tools for measuring the quality of \nhigher education.\nBeyond higher education, the study of learning engagement has expanded across \nvarious educational stages. In 2002, the National Center for Education Statistics launched the Education Longitudinal Study of 2002 (ELS:2002) [ 18]. ELS:2002 is \na large-scale longitudinal study aimed at tracking a representative cohort of stu -\ndents, beginning in high school and continuing to collect data on their educational, career, and social developments. The study involved approximately 15,000 students from the tenth grade of 2002, ensuring national representation across different racial, gender, socioeconomic, and geographic backgrounds. Through multiple waves of data collection, ELS:2002 offers an in-depth analysis of students’ learning engagement at various stages of development, as well as its long-term impact on academic performance, higher education, and career outcomes. In contrast to the cross-sectional data provided by the NSSE, ELS:2002 offers a dynamic, longitudi -\nnal perspective, aiding researchers in understanding how learning engagement plays a role throughout a stude')
```

Revathy, S. Tamil Selvan, C. P. Thamil Selvi, and E. Angel Anna Prathiba\n1.1  INTRODUCTION\nArtificial intelligence (AI) and deep learning have rapidly altered a wide range of \nsectors, including healthcare and finance, autonomous systems, and natural language processing. The optimization process is crucial for enhancing model performance and generalization in AI systems, particularly deep neural networks. Despite major progress, optimizing sophisticated AI models remains difficult due to the intricate structure of their loss landscapes, which sometimes feature several local minima, high dimensionality, and non-convexity. Traditional optimization approaches, such as gradient descent and its derivatives (e.g., Stochastic Gradient Descent, Adam), have formed the foundation for training deep learning models. These approaches use gradient information to iteratively update model parameters in the path of the loss function’s steepest descent [ 1]. While useful for many tasks, gradient-based \napproaches encounter significant limitations:\n 1 .  L ocal Minima and Saddle Points: Deep neural networks’ loss landscapes are \noften craggy, with several local minima and saddle points. Gradient-based algorithms might become stuck at these suboptimal spots, resulting in poor model performance.\n 2 .  V anishing and Exploding Gradients: Gradients in very deep networks can \nbecome either too tiny (vanishing gradients) or too huge (exploding gradi -\nents), making good training and convergence difficult to achieve.\n 3 .  H igh-Dimensional Optimization Challenges: Deep learning models com -\nmonly embrace a great number of restrictions, making the optimization issue exceedingly high dimensional. This intricacy adds to the difficulty of identifying optimal solutions.1\n2 Advanced AI and Data Science Applications\n 4 .  N on-Convexity: The loss functions in deep learning models are highly \nnon-convex, which means there are numerous areas of the parameter space \nwhere the function is not smooth or simple to optimize.\nMetaheuristic optimization approaches offer effective solutions to these problems. Metaheuristic algorithms, unlike gradient-based approaches, do not require gradient information and are intended to efficiently explore the solution space. They draw inspiration from natural processes, biological evolution, and swarm behaviors and provide various advantages.\nGenetic algorithms (GAs) are founded on the ideas of natural collection and \ngenetics. They employ techniques including selection, crossover, and mutation to evolve a population of solutions across several generations. GAs are very beneficial for tweaking hyperparameters and optimizing neural network design. Their capacity to traverse broad search areas while maintaining variety among solutions aids in avoiding local minima.\nParticle Swarm Optimization (PSO) models social behavior in birds and fish. In \nPSO, a swarm of candidate solutions (particles) moves around the search space, mod -\nifying their locations depending on their own best-known positions as well as those of their neighbors. This collaborative technique strikes a balance between explora -\ntion and exploitation, making it beneficial for refining weights and hyperparameters in neural networks.\nSimulated Annealing (SA) is similar to the annealing process in metallurgy, \nwhich involves gradually cooling materials to obtain stability. SA searches the solu -\ntion space by tolerating inferior answers on a probabilistic scale that diminishes with time. SA is well suited for complicated optimization issues due to its ability to escape local minima and converge to global optima.\nAnt Colony Optimization (ACO) is based on ants’ foraging behavior. It employs \npheromone trails to direct the search process, with solutions dependent on the strength of pheromone deposits. ACO is useful for combinatorial optimization issues and can optimize network architecture and scheduling jobs.\nJaya Optimization is a modern technique that is both simple and effective. It \ndirects candidate solutions toward the best-found answers while avoiding the worst options. Jaya Optimization does not require any specified parameters, making it sim -\nple to apply and applicable to a wide range of optimization issues.\nFirefly Optimization is modeled by the flashing characteristic of fireflies, which \nattracts other fireflies. This technique aids in studying multimodal functions and complicated environments. Firefly Optimization is particularly beneficial for dealing with situations involving many optima and increasing convergence rates.\n1.1.1  Significance  of ai M odel Training\nThe use of metaheuristic optimization approaches for AI model training has various \nadvantages:\nEnhanced Exploration: Metaheuristic algorithms provide more thorough \nexploration of the solution space, lowering the danger of becoming trapped in local minima.\n3 Solving Complex AI Model Training Challenges\nRobustness: These strategies can handle non-differentiable and irregular objective \nfunctions, which are prevalent in complicated artificial intelligence models.\nFlexibility: They may be used in a variety of optimization issues such as \n hyperparameter tweaking, network architecture design, and deep learning \nmodel training.\n1.1.2  MoTivaTion and objec TiveS\nGiven the limits of standard optimization approaches and the promise of meta -\nheuristic techniques, the purpose of this work is to inspect and assess the efficacy \nof various metaheuristic algorithms in AI model training. We want to uncover the strengths, limits, and ideal applications of genetic algorithms, Particle Swarm Opti -\nmization, Simulated Annealing, Ant Colony Optimization, Jaya Optimization, and Firefly Optimization [ 2]. This study’s findings will give important insights into how \nto improve AI model performance and efficiency using sophisticated optimization methodologies.\n1.2  LITERATURE SURVEY\nSurrogated models improve metaheuristic optimization by lowering computing costs in AI model training, which aids in effectively tackling complicated tasks, as men-tioned in the study article. The results show that using an adaptive strategy using surrogate models enhances convergence in fitness functions, and BPS dynamically picks the best performing surrogate during the optimization process [ 3].\nMetaheuristic optimization algorithms are useful tools for addressing difficult \noptimization issues, such as AI model training challenges, by balancing explora -\ntion and exploitation without relying on specific gradients. The approaches described include solution selection: Critical judgments on improving or developing chosen solutions and evaluation with the objective function, iteratively quantifying solution performance. The findings investigate the essential components and principles of metaheuristic optimization algorithms.\nDisplays graphic representations of the search behavior of specified metaheuristic \nalgorithms [ 4].\nFirefly algorithms, Particle Swarm Optimization, and Ant Colony Optimization \nare examples of metaheuristic strategies to tune hyperparameters for deep learning models, efficiently solving challenging AI model training issues. The approaches are firefly algorithms, PSO, and Ant Colony Optimization, applied to improve the performance of CNN models in deep learning. The results are that metaheuristic approaches improve the performance of deep learning models; however, applying metaheuristics to deep learning models presents challenges [ 5].\nMetaheuristic algorithms, such as Variable Neighborhood Search, provide unique \nways for optimizing Artificial Neural Network parameters, solving the issues of effectively training complicated AI models. Metaheuristic algorithms and a Variable Neighborhood Search (VNS) methodology were applied. The result is a Variable Neighborhood Search technique for ANN parameter optimization [ 6].\nMetaheuristic algorithms improve neural networks and deep learning by \neffectively dealing with complicated training issues, providing advantages over \n4 Advanced AI and Data Science Applications\ngradient-based approaches in issues of investigation, exploitation, and dodging local \nminima. Metaheuristic algorithms and gradient-based back propagation techniques were employed. The study presents a thorough examination of the use of MH algo -\nrithms to optimize ANNs and deep learning models. It categorizes the most recent MH algorithms in the literature to demonstrate their efficacy in DL and ANN train -\ning for a variety of applications [ 7].\nMetaheuristic algorithms improve AI model training by rapidly searching solu-\ntion spaces for global optimums, providing iterative improvements via fitness func -\ntions and heuristic search techniques. Metaheuristic optimization and local search approaches were applied. The re')

Risk Science: An Introduction explores the the ‑\nory and practice of risk science, providing concepts and tools for understanding and \nacting under conditions of uncertainty.\nThe chapters in this book cover the fundamental concepts, principles, approaches, \nmethods and models for how to understand, assess, communicate, manage and govern risk. These topics are presented and examined in a way which details how they relate, for example, how to characterize and communicate risk with particular emphasis on reflecting uncertainties; how to distinguish risk perception and professional risk judg ‑\nments; how to assess risk and guide decision makers, especially for cases involving large uncertainties and value differences; and how to integrate risk assessment with resilience‑based strategies. The text provides a variety of examples and case studies that relate to highly visible and relevant issues facing risk academics, practitioners and non‑risk leaders who must make risk‑related decisions.\nThis revised and updated second edition features an entirely new chapter on the \nintegrity and quality of risk studies, and dealing with misinformation in the context of risk. Presenting both the foundational and most recent advancements in the subject matter, this work particularly suits students of risk science courses at college and uni ‑\nversity level. The book also provides broader key reading for students and scholars in other domains, including business, engineering and public health.\nTerje Aven is Professor of Risk Analysis and Risk Science at the University of Stavanger, \nNorway.\nShital Thekdi is Professor of Analytics and Operations at the University of Richmond, \nUSA.Risk Science\n\nRisk Science\nAn Introduction\nSECOND EDITION\nTerje Aven and Shital Thekdi\nSecond edition published 2025\nby Routledge4 Park Square, Milton Park, Abingdon, Oxon, OX14 4RN\nand by Routledge\n605 Third Avenue, New York, NY 10158\nRoutledge is an imprint of the Taylor & Francis Group, an informa business© 2025 Terje Aven and Shital ThekdiThe right of Terje Aven and Shital Thekdi to be identified as authors of this work \nhas been asserted in accordance with sections 77 and 78 of the Copyright, Designs and Patents Act 1988.\nAll rights reserved. Previously, he was Professor (adjunct) in Risk Analysis at the University of Oslo and the Norwegian University of Science and Technology. He has many years of experience as a risk analyst in industry and as a consultant. He is the author of many books and papers in the field, covering both fundamental issues as well as practical risk analysis methods. He has led several large research programs in the risk area, with strong international participation. He has developed many master programs in the field and has lectured on many courses in risk analysis and risk man‑agement. Aven is Editor‑in‑Chief of the Journal of Risk and Reliability and Area Editor of Risk Analysis in Policy. He has served as President of the International Society for \nRisk Analysis (SRA) and Chairman of the European Safety and Reliability Association (ESRA) (2014–2018).\nShital Thekdi\xa0is a Professor of Analytics and Operations at the University of Richmond, \nUSA. She has co‑authored several papers on risk management and decision‑making. She has a Ph.D. in Systems & Information Engineering from the University of Virginia; she has an M.S.E. and a B.S.E. in Industrial & Operations Engineering from the University of Michigan. She has several years of experience working in industry, with extensive supply chain management and operational analytics experience.About the authors\nThis is a textbook on risk science\xa0–\xa0covering the basic concepts, principles, approaches, \nmethods and models for how to understand, assess, communicate, manage and govern risk. It presents the foundational and most recent advancements in the subject matter for a risk science course at the university/college level while applying these to recent high‑profile risk‑related events and issues. It is relevant for students from all types of domains, including business, engineering and public health, as it highlights the generic and fundamental concepts, principles, approaches, methods and models of this science and field. In order to adequately handle risk, it is essential to understand all the core subjects of risk science and how they relate, for example, what is risk science; how to characterize and communicate risk, with particular emphasis on reflecting uncertain‑ties; how to distinguish risk judgments and risk perception; how to assess risk and guide decision makers, especially for cases involving large uncertainties and value dif‑ferences; and how to integrate risk assessment with resilience‑based strategies.\nWe encounter risk in all types of situations, such as in our personal lives and when \nconsidering high‑level issues like climate change and politics. At a firm level, an organi‑zation invests in a project with a goal to be profitable; in reality, this project could result in profitability or failure and losses. At a global health level, the potential occurrence of a health pandemic represents a risk for societies and activities all over the world. Today we do not know what types of epidemics that will happen in coming years, how they will develop or what the consequences will be.\nRisk is a concept that is fundamental to our lives, our work and our societies. The \nessence of the concept is that events may occur in the future that are not as planned or anticipated. Being subject to risk means that there is a potential for some undesirable consequences of these events. The actual consequences could, however, be positive. For example, following a major investment, the firm could be profitable. Without risk, little happens. As the saying goes, who dares nothing, need hope for nothing. The saying is just as relevant to individuals as it is to businesses and society. On the other hand, we also want to eliminate and reduce many types of risk. We do not want the food we eat to increase the risk of cancer, the health risk in relation to an epidemic should be as low as possible and jobs should be safe. Taking risks implies that mistakes or accidents can occur. These failures can eventually result in learning and improvement over time, but too high a risk obviously can be disastrous. The challenge is to find the right balance Preface to the first edition\nix PREFACE TO THE FIRST EDITION  \nbetween creating value and taking risk on the one hand and protecting and reducing \nrisk on the other. We strive to find this balance, as individuals, businesses and societies at large.\nRisk science can help us find this balance and handle the risk in a good way. This \nrequires that we be able to understand what risks we are facing. We must be able to dis‑tinguish between what is worth worrying about and what is not. For example, is it safe to travel by air? Can we ignore the risks related to an emerging epidemic if we imple‑ment the measures recommended by health organizations? Does investment in a spe‑cific project involve too high a risk? Risk science will help you and your organization respond prudently. It clarifies how we should conduct risk assessments. It emphasizes that we must be careful not to mix professional risk assessments with ‘risk perception’ that reflects feelings such as fear and dread. For example, many people do not like to travel by plane. For many of them, the feelings\xa0–\xa0the risk perception\xa0–\xa0explain their behavior. Risk science clarifies and discusses what professional risk assessments are and what risk perception is. It may lead to the conclusion that you will confront the risk, as the problem is largely about fear and dread and not high risk.\nHowever, risk science does not prescribe what decision to make. For example, trav‑\nelers must make the choice whether to fly. They must consider the cost of the trip, the risks and the benefit of the flight and any other factors, such as environmental consid‑erations. Risk science helps structure the thinking and perform the assessments so that the ‘right’ decision can be made under the current circumstances.\nAs another example, consider the early stage of an emerging new type of epidemic. \nNo objective scientific answers can be derived because of the large uncertainties. Yet risk science can provide informative risk characterizations and support the develop‑ment of suitable policies for handling the epidemic. The policies may include weights given to precautionary measures, as there is a potential for severe losses, and the con‑sequences of the epidemic are subject to scientific uncertainties. Risk science explains what a precautionary approach is about and how it relates to different concerns, in particular the need for developments and creating values.\nTo guide decision‑making, economists recommend the use of cost‑benefit analy‑\nsis. Risk science explains how these analyses should be applied in a risk context and combined with risk assessments to provide even a better decision basis. To provide a response to the issue about a project investment involving too high a risk, the risks need to be identified, analyzed and understood. Risk science provides concepts and tools for this purpose. Basic questions addressed in risk science and risk assessments are: What might go wrong? Why and how might it go wrong? And what are the consequences? What do we know, and what do we not know? On the basis of such knowledge, we see better what is required, what measures are needed and how we should proceed. Is it possible to avoid some risks? Can we control the risks if something surprising happens? Are the systems and organizations involved sufficiently robust and resilient in case something happens, such as a disturbance or change of any kind, even not foreseen?\nRisk science is very much concerned about such issues, to understand the risks \nand how the risks relate to robustness and resilience. A main strategy of risk handling is adopting robustness and resilience‑based thinking and measures. Risk assessments \nx    PREFACE TO THE FIRST EDITION\nalone cannot guide us in how to deal with risk in situations of large uncertainties. \nThese assessments have weaknesses and limitations, and some aspects of risk may be overlooked. To meet potential surprises, we need to think resilience. Risk science guides us in how to do this and integrate risk and resilience‑based analysis and management.\nEven if we all agree on the magnitude of the risks, we may have different views \non what to do about the risks, as we have different values and priorities. How much weight to put on the uncertainties, for example, in relation to climate change, is a value judgment. Resilience is a key strategy to meeting uncertainties but so are dialogue and participation. Because of the uncertainties, there is no objective best approach to handle the risks. Different stakeholders weigh the various concerns differently. Risk science explains how dialogue and public involvement processes can reveal existing positions and perspectives and lead to improved understanding among different stakeholders, which in its turn can lead to broad support for a specific risk policy and risk‑handling approach.\nOBJECTIVES\nThe main objectives of the book are to present concepts, principles and tools for under ‑\nstanding and acting under conditions of uncertainty and risk. This is becoming increas‑ingly important as businesses, policymakers and public‑sector leaders are tasked with decision‑making and investment using varying levels of information and knowledge. The major categories of topic interest are risk assessment, risk perception and commu‑nication and risk management (which here also covers risk governance and risk policy). These terms are fully defined and explored in the text, addressing features such as:\nRisk assessment: People and organizations generally begin to understand risk by \ninvestigating potential risk events and gauging the severity or urgency of acting on those events and risks. Following the risk assessment process would provide a structure to answering questions such as:\n•\n What are the risk events of concern? \nWhy? What are the consequences of these \nevents? What are the main uncertainties?\n•\n How does the availability of information and knowledge influence the assessment \nof the risk?\nRisk perception and communication:\n Professionals assess risk, but for proper under ‑\nstanding, communication and handling of risk, it is critical also to study how people perceive the risks. The perceptions reflect the real concerns of people and may include important aspects of risk not fully captured by risk assessments. There is often a need to communicate aspects of a risk with various audiences, such as the general public, the media and other stakeholders. In this setting, a person or organization needs to build on risk science and be intentional in communicating with stakeholders, ensuring an appropriate tone, including the necessary information, providing a clear message and encouraging an appropriate response.\nxi   PREFACE TO THE FIRST EDITION  \nRisk management: Following a risk assessment process, people and organizations \nmust decide how to act, such as asking the following questions:\n•\n How does the availability of information and knowledge influence how I (we) \nshould address the \nrisk? Should we strengthen the knowledge base before making \na decision?\n•\n Do we need to act now to confront the risk?\n•\n Do we need to invest in anything to protect lives or some other values? If so,\n what \nresources can or should we invest?\n•\n Do we need insurance?\n•\n What is an acceptable risk level? How safe is safe enough?\n•\n How much risk should we take in pursuit of values?\n•\n What policies should be implemented in order to best handle the risks?\n•\n What \nstructures for decision‑making authority and accountability should be used?\nThe topics described previously apply to students and of all domains and expertise \n levels.\n We acknowledge that some students may pursue entire careers related to risk \nand safety. Others may be seeking (or be currently working in) careers that are not directly devoted to these areas. This book is aimed at both types of students. The text and the case studies will demonstrate that roles across domain areas involve decision‑making under conditions of uncertainty and risk. For example, healthcare professionals must decide on the most appropriate treatment for a patient, recogniz‑ing uncertainties related to the effectiveness of the treatment, the accuracy of the diagnosis and variability that exists within biological or human mechanisms dictating the patient’s response. As another example, supply chain professionals must decide on the most appropriate response to natural disasters, recognizing uncertainties existing in the timing, geographic location and severity of the disaster itself.\nThis book is written at an introductory level with a focus on fundamental concepts \nand application of those concepts without compromising stringency and precision. While this book is designed to be accessible to students with minimal background in mathematics, students will benefit from having some basic understanding of probabil‑ity theory and statistics. In general, the book is, however, very much self‑contained. The key terms and concepts will be carefully introduced and explained within the text. To make the book accessible and practical for students across domains, there is particular emphasis on using an interactive and engaging format for comprehension activities. These activities include written exercises and presentations. A number of exercises, with answers, are included.\nThe main target group for the book is students, but the book should also be of \ninterest for many others, including managers, professionals and scientists working with risk issues related to, for example, engineering, business and public health, who would like to get an overview of the core subjects of risk science.\nAs risk science covers a wide variety of topics and issues, it is impossible to cover \nthem all. There will always be a discussion about what in fact the state‑of‑the‑art con‑cepts, principles, approaches, methods and models of a science are. The present book \nxii\nbuilds on recent work conducted by the Society for Risk Analysis (SRA 2015, 2017a, \n2017b) and related research, which point to key risk science terminology, core sub‑jects and fundamental principles. These documents have been developed by a group of senior risk scientists with different backgrounds and competences. The subjectivity in topics, issues and examples highlighted in this book is, however, acknowledged. For further studies of the scientific basis of the present book, see Aven (2018a, 2020a) and Aven and Thekdi (2020).\nRisk science is the most updated and justified knowledge on risk fundamentals \n(concepts), risk assessment, risk perception and communication and risk man ‑\nagement (including risk governance and policies on risk). This book aims at pre ‑\nsenting the core of this knowledge. Risk science is also about the process\xa0–\xa0the \n p\nractice\xa0–\xa0that gives us this knowledge. The present book also discusses to some \nextent this aspect of risk science, but its main scope and focus are on presenting the current knowledge on risk fundamentals (concepts), risk assessment, risk per ‑\nception and communication and risk management.\nCONTENTS\nChapter 1 introduces three main cases which will be used throughout the book to \nillustrate the concepts, principles, approaches, methods and models presented. These cases relate to the personal, organizational and societal level, respectively. Case 1 dis‑cusses risk issues for a student in an academically rigorous environment; Case 2\xa0looks into a company considering supply chain investments in response to major potential changes in business and technology; and Case 3\xa0addresses a public health crisis, a global pandemic.\nThe following chapters of the book are organized into five parts. Part I covers basic \nconcepts, Part II risk assessment, Part III risk perception and communication, Part IV risk management and Part V tackling practical risk problems and issues.\nThe aim of Part I is to motivate and define the basic concepts of risk science. The \nmain focus is on the risk term, but we also discuss related terms, including probabil‑ity, uncertainty, knowledge, vulnerability, resilience, safety and security. A distinction is made between the concept of risk (Chapter 2) and how risk is measured, described or characterized (Chapter 3). We face risk when we drive a car without consciously measuring or describing the level of the risk. We can also make a similar dichotomy for other terms, like vulnerability and resilience.\nPart II covers risk assessment. Chapter 4 reviews basic theory, including scope and \naims of risk assessment and stages and processes in a risk assessment: planning, hazard (threat, opportunity) identification, cause analysis and consequence analysis. It also dis‑cusses the quality of risk assessments and challenges in relation to their use. Examples of risk assessment methods are outlined and followed up in Chapter 5, which reviews basic methods such as sensitivity and importance analysis, Bayesian networks, Monte    PREFACE TO THE FIRST EDITION\nxiii\nCarlo simulation, probabilistic risk assessment (quantitative risk assessment)\xa0–\xa0PRA/\nQRA\xa0–\xa0and specific methods for analyzing vulnerability and resilience. There exist a number of risk assessment methods, some general and relevant for all types of situa‑tions, others more tailored to meet specific application needs. We highlight the former category but also present some specific methods and models, in particular related to safety applications.\nThe aim of Part III is to review basic knowledge about risk perception (Chapter 6) \nand risk communication (Chapter 7). Topics of Chapter 6 include risk perception and emotions/affects; the meaning of the two modes of thought, System 1 and System 2, and their link to risk perception and risk assessments; differences between expert and non‑expert judgments of risk; factors shaping risk perception; social amplification of risk; and the relationship between risk perception and trust.\nRisk communication is about how exchange or sharing of risk‑related data, infor ‑\nmation and knowledge between and among different parties (such as regulators, experts, consumers, media, general public) can be provided. Chapter 7 discusses various topics related to risk communication, including the different functions and goals of risk com‑munication, the importance of building risk communication on risk science, key ideas and features of basic models and theories of risk communication, what characterizes good risk communication messages and common methods for developing and testing risk communication messages.\nPart IV covers fundamentals of risk management, including risk governance issues \nand policies on risk. Chapter 8\xa0addresses topics such as the basic strategies and policies for handling risk, the main elements of the risk management process, the use of risk acceptance and tolerability criteria in risk handling, the meaning and use of the caution‑ary and precautionary principles and the importance of vulnerability and resilience.\nChapter 9 reviews related tools, including utility theory, cost‑benefit analyses and \nmulti‑attribute analyses. In particular, the chapter discusses the concepts of value of a statistical life (VSL) and implied cost of averting a fatality (ICAF).\nPart V discusses the tackling of practical problems and issues by integrating theo‑\nries and methods from the other categories of topics (parts). Chapter 10 covers general insights and experiences. Three topics are highlighted in this chapter: (1) competences: to understand risk and solve risk problems in practice, broad risk analysis and risk sci‑ence competences are required. It is not enough to rely on knowledge gained through standards and applied certification arrangements. (2) Pre‑analysis and framing issues, which relate to, inter alia, the objectives of the analysis and use of standards. (3) Treat‑ment and communication of uncertainties, which is a main challenge in using risk analysis in practice. Chapter 11\n1 returns to the three cases of Chapter 1. In the student \nexample, we focus on the planning, execution and use of a risk assessment. In the enter ‑\nprise example, we look more closely into the process of integrating performance, risk and vulnerability (resilience) analysis and management. In the social case, we highlight issues linked to science, policies and politics.\n1 This is Chapter 12 in this second edition of the book.   PREFACE TO THE FIRST EDITION  \nxiv\nIn addition, the book includes three appendices, on terminology (Appendix A), basic \nrisk science (Appendix B) and a summary of answers to problems and exercises through‑\nout the book. Appendix B reviews basic knowledge about risk science; how it is defined; and what the difference is between generic, fundamental risk science and applied risk science. The former category covers scientific knowledge on generic issues like ‘What is risk?’ and ‘How should risk best be described?’, whereas the latter \n category covers scien\n‑\ntific risk knowledge related to specific applications, for example, on safety and security issues for a concrete activity. Appendix B also discusses educational programs in the area.\nThe title of this book is Risk Science: An Introduction. It reflects that the aim is to \npresent an overview of the most updated and justified knowledge of this science on risk fundamentals (concepts), risk assessment, risk perception and communication and risk management and governance (Chapters 1–11). This knowledge can be used to under ‑\nstand, assess, communicate and handle risk in practical situations, such as for climate change risks, business risks and health risks. Risk science is also about the process\xa0–\xa0the practice\xa0–\xa0that gives us this knowledge. This book also discusses to some extent this aspect of risk science, but its main scope and focus are on presenting current knowl‑edge on risk fundamentals (concepts), risk assessment, risk perception and communica‑tion and risk management. For the risk science practice aspect, several examples are presented. Appendix B provides a general background text for clarifying the meaning of these risk science concepts. However, the following chapters can be read without a deeper understanding of these concepts, keeping in mind that the chapters simply pro‑vide state‑of‑the‑art knowledge on risk fundamentals (concepts), risk assessment, risk perception and communication and risk management\xa0–\xa0and they include some illustra‑tions of the risk science knowledge generation process (practice).\nThe book covers several features that facilitate the learning and application of \nrisk science. The features include (1) a summary at the start of each chapter explain‑ing what the coming chapter is about, which includes a list of learning objectives; (2) the highlighting of new terms and definitions introduced, as well as key points made; (3) a summary of key symbols; (4) many examples and figures throughout the book to illustrate the theories studied; (5) a number of questions (reflection boxes) through‑out the text to stimulate the reader to reflect on the concepts, principles, approaches, methods and models discussed; (6) a review at the end of each chapter highlighting the most important ideas and features of the chapter (key takeaways); and (7) problems at the end of each chapter, with answers (Appendix C). For some of the problems, the answers extend beyond the material presented in previous sections. In this way, addi‑tional knowledge is added to the chapter.\nThe learning objectives highlight the knowledge of central concepts, principles, \napproaches, methods and models of risk analysis and risk science (covering risk assess‑ment, risk characterization, risk perception, risk communication and risk management). In addition, we can formulate some objectives related to ‘know‑how’ (skills), including\n•\n being able to identify state‑of‑the‑art concepts,\n principles, approaches, methods \nand models of risk analysis and risk science   PREFACE TO THE FIRST EDITION\nxv\n• being able to use basic concepts,  principles, approaches, methods and models in \nrisk analysis and risk science settings\n•\n being able to support the tackling of risk problems using contemporary risk analy\n‑\nsis and science\nAs a concrete example, think about the skill of being able to conduct a risk assess‑\nment. The book provides basic knowledge (‘know‑that’) for obtaining this skill using theory and illustrative examples, but considerable training beyond studying this book is needed to be a competent risk assessor conducting professional risk assessments in industry, business and so on.   PREFACE TO THE FIRST EDITION  \nPreface to the second \nedition\nIn this second edition of the book, a new chapter, Chapter 11, on integrity and quality \nof risk studies has been added to the original version. The important topic of misin‑formation in relation to risk is given special attention. In Chapter 11, we present a recently developed classification system to evaluate quality and integrity of risk studies and their supporting evidence. The chapter presents and discusses different categories of misinformation. These categories can be viewed as a checklist for issues to consider when evaluating and discussing the quality of a risk studies.\nIn addition, we have made a number of minor adjustments to correct misprints, \nupdate references and improve the presentation.\nWe have received positive feedback to the first edition from colleagues, students \nand friends. Their hints and suggestions have been incorporated into this second edi‑tion to the extent possible. We thank all who have given feedback, to help us improve the book.\nTerje Aven and Shital Thekdi\nFebruary 1, 2024\nWe are grateful to Enrico Zio, Seth Guikema, Ortwin Renn, Roger Flage, E')

Their wisdom and passion for cloud governance continue \nto echo through these pages.\nThis book is also dedicated to the memory of Ronald Linthicum (1939–2014).  \nDavid’s accomplishments were made with Ronald’s encouragement and guid -\nance.\n\nForeword\nIn every era of technological transformation, there comes a time when optimism \noutpaces preparedness. Today, as organizations race toward the promise of the \nAI-enabled cloud, many find themselves caught between the limitless potential \nand the sobering complexity of modern digital ecosystems. This book, Unlocking \nthe Power of the Cloud , is a timely and essential compass for navigating that ter-\nrain. It doesn’t simply celebrate the cloud’s possibilities—it demands that we con-\nfront its realities.\nI have spent decades observing, building, and advising on enterprise technol -\nogies. And never before have I seen such profound shifts in the way we think \nabout value, risk, governance, and accountability as I have with the intersection \nof cloud computing and artificial intelligence. We’ve entered a period where the \ncloud is no longer just an IT infrastructure decision. It is now a board-level con-\nversation, a strategic differentiator, and a source of competitive risk—all at once.\nMeredith Stein and David Linthicum understand this intersection deeply. \nThey know that the cloud’s allure—cost savings, scalability, innovation—is real \nbut not guaranteed. They also recognize that cloud adoption isn’t a destination; \nit’s a perpetual journey, shaped by evolving technologies, shifting regulatory land -\nscapes, and human factors. This is not a how-to manual. It’s something much \nmore important: a governance framework for the age of disruption.\nOne of the book’s core strengths lies in its refusal to reduce cloud governance \nto technical checklists. Instead, Stein and Linthicum examine cloud transforma -\ntion as a “wicked problem”—one that defies simple definitions, clear answers, or \nlinear solutions. They call on leaders to grapple with organizational culture, \ncross-functional accountability, and long-term strategy, not just cloud vendors \nand architecture. The book challenges decision-makers to ask tougher questions \nand to develop adaptive, context-aware governance mechanisms.\nIt’s also refreshing to see how the authors confront the mythology of the \ncloud. Chapter by chapter, they deconstruct the idea of a seamless, utopian future \npowered by cloud and AI. They offer instead a more balanced view: one where \nreal gains are possible—but only with intentional governance, active risk man -\nagement, and sustained investment in people, process, and policy. They name the \nelephants in the room: data breaches, unaccountable shared responsibilities, \noverhyped vendor promises, and the environmental consequences of digital in-\nfrastructure. In doing so, they provide a voice of reason in a sea of marketing hy-\nperbole.\nThis book’s structure reflects its core belief that cloud governance is a living \necosystem. Beginning with foundational principles and moving through perfor -\nmance, compliance, cybersecurity, and board oversight, Stein and Linthicum \nguide readers through a comprehensive framework. Their “Cloud Governance \nHouse” metaphor elegantly organizes the moving parts of cloud strategy, showing \nhow each function—from audit to change management—must work together \nunder one roof. It is a model not just for IT leaders, but for C-suites and directors \nwho need to align digital ambition with fiduciary duty.\nAnother reason this book stands out is its focus on actionable insight. Every \nchapter ends with a “Call to Action”—a thoughtful set of steps that organizations \ncan begin applying today. Whether it’s conducting a cloud security audit, map -\nping shared responsibility models, or building an inventory of cloud assets, these \nactions are practical, grounded, and essential.\nMoreover, the authors are unafraid to look to the future. They explore emer -\ngent technologies like Quantum Computing, Agentic AI, and Cloud Gaming—not \nas novelties, but as inevitable forces reshaping risk, regulation, and enterprise ar-\nchitecture. Their analysis of sustainability, cyber insurance gaps, and the board’s \ndigital literacy is especially prescient. These are not issues we can defer; they are \nalready affecting how organizations are judged by regulators, investors, and cus-\ntomers.\nIt’s important to note that this book is not just for technologists. It speaks to a \nmuch wider audience: boards of directors, CFOs, compliance officers, HR leaders, \nand strategists. In an age when cloud and AI are inseparable from enterprise gov-\nernance, this is the kind of resource that every cross-functional leadership team \nshould read together. It brings shared language, shared understanding, and—per -\nhaps most importantly—shared accountability.\nIn closing, Unlocking the Power of the Cloud delivers what the title promises, \nbut with a crucial distinction: it makes clear that power is not granted automati -\ncally by technology. Power must be unlocked through responsible stewardship, \nintentional governance, and an unflinching look at both the promises and the per-\nils of the digital age.\nIf your organization is navigating cloud transformation, this book will be a \ntrusted guide. If you are responsible for ensuring that transformation is aligned \nwith your mission, values, and obligations—this book may just be indispensable.\nJo Peterson\nVice President Cloud, Security and AI-Clarify360 and Chief Analyst \nClearTech ResearchVIII Foreword\nContents\nForeword VII\nPart I: The Basics of Cloud Governance and AI-Enablement \nChapter 1 \nThe Utopian Promises of the Cloud and the AI-Driven Future 3\nChapter 2 \nThe Dark Side of the Cloud 16\nChapter 3 \nThe Basics of Cloud Computing 22\nPart II: A Look at the Context of Cloud Computing and AI \nChapter 4 \nCloud Strategy and Performance 35\nChapter 5 \nCloud-Driven Change Management and Learning 49\nPart III: Governance Disrupted and Transformed by the \nCloud \nChapter 6 \nSharing Cloud Governance Responsibilities and Integrating the Cloud \nwith Enterprise Risk Management 65\nChapter 7 \nSecurity, Trust, and the Cloud 82\nChapter 8 \nCompliance and the Cloud 96\nChapter 9 \nAudit, Assurance, and the Cloud 107\nChapter 10 \nThe Board of Directors: Asking the Right Questions about AI and the \nCloud 114\nNotes 123\nAbout the Authors 135\nList of Figures 137\nList of Tables 139\nList of Boxes 141\nIndex 143X Contents\nPart I: The Basics of Cloud Governance  \nand AI-Enablement\n\nChapter 1 \nThe Utopian Promises of the Cloud and the  \nAI-Driven Future\nUtopias have their value – nothing so wonderfully expands the imaginative horizons of \nhuman potentialities – but as guides to conduct they can prove literally fatal.1 \n– Isaiah Berlin, Oxford philosopher, historian, and author\nIntroduction\nThe utopian promises of cloud computing, compounded by the chaos of large- \nscale deployment and the resulting organizational upheaval, have profound and \nfar-reaching governance implications. The chaos of the cloud is a wicked problem\nthat creates confusion, perplexity, and risk at various points during a cloud jour -\nney. This book will explore how an organization can leverage cloud governance \nto moderate the extremes, striving to attain the achievable promises of the cloud, \non the one hand, and successfully navigating and governing the chaos of the \ncloud on the other hand.\nUtopian Promises vs Realities of Cloud\nDon’t celebrate promises, and don’t fear threats.2 \n– Sicilian Proverb\nThe Oxford Learner’s Dictionary defines utopia as “an imaginary place or state \nin which everything is perfect.” Ernst Bloch, author of The Principle of Hope, sug-\ngests that imagining the achievement of utopian-conditions is an important catalyst \nfor change. In the context of the cloud, organizations have universally embraced \nthe promises and are willing to digitally transform to achieve a cloud-driven \nutopia .\nCloud vendors, academics, influencers, and media have contributed to defin -\ning and signaling the wide range of transformational benefits available through \nthe cloud. That is, compelling promises of ideal performance, innovation, produc -\ntivity, cost savings, and much more. Table 1.1presents examples of such utopian- \npromises of the cloud.\nMcKinsey found, and the authors agree, that “the value cloud generates from en-\nabling businesses to innovate is worth more than five times what is possible by \nsimply reducing IT costs.” See the call out box for a cloud governance success story \nabout a fictious organization that migrated their legacy systems to the cloud.\nAs the cloud enters a more mature phase and Artificial Intelligence is the most \nsignificant technological disruption in decades, an essential question emerges , Does \nthe cloud deliver on its utopian promises? It depends.\nAs with any disruptive and transformational change undertaken by an orga -\nnization, the level of success depends on a range of complex variables. One vari-\nable influencing cloud success is the inherent and inevitable chaos of the cloud. \nErnst Bloch stated that “Utopias are seen as impossible.”3 This perspective seems \nreasonably relevant to the cloud. Some organizations expect to achieve the uto-\npian promises of the cloud but fall short because of the inherent chaos of the \ncloud. The Cambridge Dictionary defines chaos as “A state of total confusion with \nno order.”4\nIs chaos a reality of the cloud? According to Gartner, the following gaps have \nemerged between the cloud’s promises and the chaotic-reality of an organiza -\ntion’s cloud journey. Gartner identified the gaps between:\n– An organization’s aspirations and the ability of in-house staff to execute\n– Expected gains from cloud versus the reality of what cloud can deliver\n– An organization’s business strategy and its cloud strategy\n– Existing and required operating model, or the existing and required founda -\ntional practices such as governance, “compliance and security”5Table 1.1: Examples of Utopian Promises of the Cloud. Source: Authors.\nCategories of the Utopian Promises of the AI-Enabled Cloud\nSubstantial Cost Reduction | Significant Return on Investment (ROI) \n– Lowers capital investments, in-house hardware, software, and IT maintenance\n– Facilitates increased revenue through innovation and improved customer engagement\n– Rapidly automates manual processes and enables innovation\nSweeping, Accelerated Gains in Productivity, Efficiency, Innovation, Customer Service \n– Enables productive and efficient Democratized IT employees who can collaborate and access \ndata in the cloud\n– Provides access to cutting edge technologies because of the AI-enabled cloud that can \ntransform IT-capacity (e.g., data storage, IT scaling opportunities, quantum computing)\nReduced Risk | Improved IT Governance \n– Enhances compliance with regulatory guidance by leveraging AI\n– Reduces risk to security with utilization of AI to respond to cyber threats\n– Decreases carbon footprint4 Chapter 1The Utopian Promises of the Cloud and the AI-Driven Future\nTable 1.2presents the authors’ list of the chaotic realities of the cloud.\nOrganizations strive to capitalize on the compelling promises of the cloud. At the \nsame time, failing to recognize and control the chaotic realities of the cloud cre-\nates risks to strategy, performance, and cloud governance. An organization’s jour -\nney towards cloud adoption will not be a straight line; the path will be filled with \ntwists and turns – risks and opportunities. Cloud governance plays a crucial role \nmoderating the extremes, striving to attain the achievable promises of the cloud, \non the one hand, and successfully navigating and governing the chaos of the \ncloud on the other hand.Table 1.2: Top 25 List of the Chaotic Realities of the Cloud, Source: Authors.\nChaos of the Cloud – Top ��(random order)\n1. Convoluted and complicated SLA and CSP contractsUtopian Promises vs Realities of Cloud 5\nIs the Cloud a Wicked Problem?\nWe cannot solve our problems with the same \nthinking we used when we created them.6 \n– Albert Einstein, Mathematician and Physicist\nWicked problems differ from ordinary problems as,\nWicked problems often crop up when organizations have to face constant change or unprec -\nedented challenges. They occur in a social context; the greater the disagreement among \nstakeholders, the more wicked the problem. In fact, it’s the social complexity of wicked \nproblems as much as their technical difficulties that make them tough to manage. Not all \nproblems are wicked; confusion, discord, and lack of progress are telltale signs that an issue \nmight be wicked.7\nFor this cloud governance book, the term “wicked” refers to Horst Rittel’s formal \ndefinition of a problem that is difficult to describe, has many root causes, and has \nno correct answer.8 It is not the definition for malicious intent or moral wrongdo -\ning. In a business strategy context, the Harvard Business Review identified five \ncommon characteristics of a wicked problem.\n1. The problem involves many stakeholders with different values and priorities.\n2. The issue’s roots are complex and tangled.\n3. The problem is difficult to come to grips with and changes with every attempt \nto address it.\n4. The challenge has no precedent.\n5. There’s nothing to indicate the right answer to the problem.9\nHow an organization goes about governing a wicked problem may differ in some \nimportant ways as compared to an ordinary or difficult problem. This book ex-\nplores the question, Is the cloud a wicked problem? What do you think?\nCloud Governance Ecosystem\nThe first law of ecology is that everything is related to everything else.10 \n– Barry Commoner, Biologist and Educator\nThis book focuses on cloud governance concepts and real-world practices. Table 1.3\npresents the touchstone definition of cloud governance selected for this book.\nThis definition captures the business-side and the IT-side of cloud strategy \nand, therefore, corporate and cloud governance. Moreover, this definition inte-\ngrates some of the crucial enablers of effective cloud governance. For instance, 6 Chapter 1The Utopian Promises of the Cloud and the AI-Driven Future\naccountability; responsibility (e.g., policies); strategy (e.g., value); risk manage -\nment, environmental and IT context influences; fit-for-purpose (e.g., adequate re-\nsources); and stakeholder trust.\nLeveraging this definition of cloud governance as a springboard, this book cre-\nated a high-level conceptualization of a cloud governance ecosystem . The topics in \neach chapter of this book represent the individual and interconnected components \nof a cloud governance ecosystem. Source: Thuraisingham.11\nDefinition of Cloud Governance\nCloud computing governance is a view of IT governance focused on accountability, defining decision \nrights and balancing benefit or value, risk, and resources in an environment embracing cloud \ncomputing.\nCloud computing governance creates business-driven policies and principles that establish the \nappropriate degree of investments and control around the lifecycle process for cloud computing \nservices.\nBasics of Cloud Computing\nChapter 3Cloud Strategy & Performance\nChapter 4\nChange Management & \nLearning\nChapter 5Enterprise Risk Management & \nShared Responsibility\nChapter 6\nBoard of Directors\nChapter 10Compliance\nChapter 8\nAudit \nChapter 9Security & Incident \nResponse\nChapter 7Dark Side \nof the \nCloud \nChapter 2\nFigure 1.1: Cloud Governance House: A Perspective on the Cloud Governance Ecosystem.  \nSource: Authors.Cloud Governance Ecosystem 7\nThe roof of the Cloud Governance House is the organization’s strategy (e.g., the \ninterrelated business strategy aligned with the IT strategy, the AI-enabled cloud \nstrategy, and performance). The rooms of the house are the cloud-shared responsi -\nbility model and the organizational governance functions (e.g., the Board, compli -\nance, audit, etc.). Advancements in technologies, particularly AI and information proc\n-\nessing (86%); robotics and automation (58%); and energy generation, storage and distribu -\ntion (41%), are also expected to be transformative. These trends are expected to have a di -\nvergent effect on jobs, driving both the fastest-growing and fastest-declining roles, and fueling demand for technology-related skills, including AI and big data, networks and cyber\n-\nsecurity and technological literacy.15\nOrganizations and CSPs are already seizing the opportunities to leverage AI- \nenabled cloud technologies and applications. Such technologies include Quantum \nComputing, Agentic AI, Cloud Gaming, and the Internet of Things (IoT).Table 1.4 (continued) \nTOE Framework Cloud Success Depends On . . .\nEnvironmental Context \nRefers to the arena in which a firm \nconducts its business1. Receptiveness to innovate and explore with Quantum and \nEdge Computing, AI, Metaverse\n2. Due diligence in assessing CSP capabilities\n3. Importance of the Cloud Shared Responsibility Model\n4. Environmental sustainability impact of the cloudThe AI-Driven Future 9\nQuantum Computing\nMcKinsey defines quantum computing as “a new approach to calculation that uses \nprinciples of fundamental physics to solve extremely complex problems very \nquickly.”16 Statista projects the market revenue has the potential to amount to USD \n93 billion by 2040.17 Table 1.5presents a summary of quantum computing use cases.\nThe authors think that a medium-sized organization is several years away from \nachieving a return on investment from quantum computing. Quantum computing \nintroduces unique governance challenges for cloud environments. Cloud gover -\nnance for quantum computing requires attention of emerging quantum regula -\ntions. For example, countries are:\n– developing cryptography standards to protect current encryption methods \nand critical infrastructure that could be vulnerable to quantum attacks\n– developing policies to classify quantum technologies as critical to national se-\ncurity to limit foreign investment or ownership of quantum companies and \nrestrict international collaborations\n– examining how laws should be updated to protect consumer data and ad-\ndress quantum computing’s ability to process and analyze large datasets\nQuantum computing also requires management’s attention to security protocols \nfor quantum key distribution and specialized access controls that consider the \nunique nature of quantum information processing. Quantum key distribution \nprotocols necessitate new governance frameworks to verify the integrity of quan -\ntum communication channels. Management may need to redesign access control \nmechanisms to incorporate the principle of quantum superposition. Superposi -\ntion enables quantum computers to solve mathematical problems more quickly \nthan classical computers. Furthermore, governance frameworks must establish \nclear boundaries between classical and quantum processing environments to \nmaintain security and compliance.Table 1.5: Example Use Cases for Quantum Computing.18,19,20\nIndustry Quantum Computing Example Use Cases\nHealthcare Examine of digital models to study temperature, metabolism, circulation\nFinancial Analyze transaction data for unusual activity and potential fraud\nInsurance Predict weather for climate models\nLogistics Optimize delivery routes\nOil & Gas Identify natural resources\nPublic Sector Predict seismic disruptions such volcanos and earthquakes10 Chapter 1The Utopian Promises of the Cloud and the AI-Driven Future\nAgentic AI\nIBM defines AI as “a technology that enables computers and machines to simulate \nhuman learning, comprehension, problem solving, decision making, creativity \nand autonomy.”21 An AI Agent is a program designed to perform tasks autono -\nmously. These agents operate independently when making decisions, allowing \nthe system to divide and conquer complex workflows with minimal human over -\nsight. The authors believe that Agentic AI holds immense promise. They leverage \ncloud resources to perform tasks, make decisions, and adapt to changing environ -\nments effectively and efficiently.22\nAgentic AI identifies patterns and learns from those patterns to adapt work -\nflows and refine strategies. Agents can make informed decisions based on the \ncontext of a given scenario, enabling them to mimic human reasoning. AI agents \nare transforming various industries by automating tasks and making informed \ndecisions. The authors believe that the deployment of AI Agents in the cloud en-\nhances their capabilities, making them more efficient and effective.\nStatista projects the market revenue has the potential to amount to USD \n47 billion by 2030.23 AI Agents are employed in various industries such as health -\ncare, customer service, retail manufacturing, and cybersecurity. AI Agents are \nrevolutionizing the healthcare industry by providing diagnostic support. They \ncan analyze radiology images, evaluate patient history, and recommend treat -\nment plans. This personalized and proactive approach significantly adds value to \npatient healthcare services. In addition, Bernard Marr, a Futurist in the fields of \nbusiness and technology, advances the idea that,\nAI agents could transform talent management by automating and enhancing various \nHuman Resource (HR) processes. From conducting initial candidate screenings and schedul -\ning interviews to managing employee onboarding and ongoing training, these agents could \nstreamline HR operations. They could also provide personalized career development advice \nto employees based on their skills, performance, and company needs.24\nThere are no requirements for AI Agents to be built or run on cloud platforms. \nHowever, the cloud is where most of the agent development and deployment is \ntaking place.25\nThe authors believes that market hype has led many to overestimate the cur-\nrent readiness of AI systems to deliver complex, autonomous capabilities at scale. \nAt the same time, businesses have underestimated the necessary investment in \ndata infrastructure, talent, and long-term financial commitment required to \nmake these systems work.26 Organizations must advance their governance frame -\nworks from static policies to incorporate dynamic and adaptive controls. These The AI-Driven Future 11\ncontrols should oversee Agentic AI while leveraging its capabilities to enhance \ncyber resilience and deliver value at an unprecedented scale and speed.\nCloud Gaming\nCloud gaming uses the connectivity of the 5G wireless network to allow the user \nto stream multimedia content from a cloud server to mobile phone devices, game \nconsoles, laptops, and tablets. Cloud-based gaming eliminates the requirement for \nusers to buy hardware and upgrade content. A high-definition cloud game re-\nquires low latency, large bandwidth, and low response time. The authors believe \nthat cloud gaming represents a transformational shift in how digital entertain -\nment is delivered and consumed, democratizing access to high-quality gaming ex-\nperiences without the traditional hardware barriers.\nMicrosoft Xbox, Nvidia GeForce Now, and Sony Play Station offer cloud gaming \nproducts. The market for gaming is massive and growing. Companies like these \nmust address governance challenges like data sovereignty, bandwidth provisioning, \nand latency to ensure a quality user experience. Statista projects the worldwide \ncloud gaming market will generate revenues of USD 18 billion by 2027.27\nIoT\nIBM defines the Internet of Things (IoT) as “a network of physical devices, ve-\nhicles, appliances, and other physical objects that are embedded with sensors, \nsoftware, and network connectivity, allowing them to collect and share data.”28 \nSelf-driving cars, smart thermostats, smart watches, and pacemakers are example \nIoT devices. Statista projects the worldwide IoT market will generate revenues of \nUSD 2.2 trillion in 2028 and there will be 32.1 billion IoT devices in place globally \nby 2033.,29,30 See the call out box for a further discussion on IoT devices.\nWith expanding cloud infrastructures to support IoT ecosystems, the authors \nbelieve that organizations encounter complexity in managing environments that \nspan multiple cloud providers, on-premises systems, and edge platforms. Differ -\nent operating systems, protocols, and management tools add to this complexity. \nSecurity teams must tackle a landscape where cloud services interact with nu-\nmerous IoT devices, e')
```

Thank you all for being an \nintegral part of this journey.aCknowledgmen Ts\nxvPreface\nReflecting on my journey through the ever-evolving world of data science, \nI’ve had the opportunity to lead and contribute to various machine \nlearning (ML) and artificial intelligence (AI) projects. These experiences \nhave sharpened my technical skills and highlighted a significant gap in \nour ecosystem: the need for a more organized and traceable approach to \ninfrastructure management.\nThe concept of infrastructure as code (IaC) and the advent of \nTerraform immediately captured my interest. IaC’s philosophy aligns with \nmy approach—rather than managing countless lines of code to detail every \naspect, you define the desired state of your resources and let Terraform, \nin conjunction with the platform, handle the rest. Terraform’s lightweight \nnature and its integration with more than 1,000 providers make it a \nversatile tool, regardless of the cloud platform your organization uses.\nTerraform has genuinely transformed my approach to infrastructure \nmanagement. It has alleviated the repetitive tasks of daily operations \nand significantly reduced the risk of human error. While mistakes are an \ninevitable part of being human, IaC and Terraform enhance our processes, \nmaking them more transparent, traceable, and reliable. Just like the book’s \ntitle suggests, Terraform has indeed made a lot of things easier!\nThis realization inspired me to explore Terraform deeply and share \nmy experiences through this book. I wholeheartedly recommend it to \ndata engineers, data scientists, machine learning engineers, and solution \narchitects. It’s not just a tool for efficiency; it’s a way to refine your \nworkflow and maintain your peace of mind in a demanding field.\nxvi What You’ll Gain from\xa0This Book\nWelcome to your comprehensive guide to mastering Terraform—the \nleading tool for IaC.\xa0Whether you’re just starting out or looking to enhance \nyour existing cloud management skills, this book is designed to elevate \nyour understanding and application of Terraform.\nHere’s what you can expect from this resource:\n• A Strong Foundation: We begin by simplifying IaC \nand Terraform, clarifying their benefits for efficiency, \ncollaboration, and consistency in infrastructure \nmanagement. You’ll grasp the core reasons behind \nadopting IaC and how it can transform your approach \nto managing infrastructure.\n• Practical, Hands-on Learning: Engage with practical \nexercises right from the start. We’ll walk you through \nthe installation of Terraform and teach you the \nessential commands—init, plan, apply, and destroy. \nWith these skills, you’ll confidently manage your \ninfrastructure using code.\n• Harnessing Terraform’s Full Potential: We’ll explore \nthe fundamental concepts that make Terraform so \neffective. You’ll become adept with providers, variables, \nmodules, and dependencies—key elements for creating \nefficient, reusable infrastructure configurations.\n• Real-World Applications: Dive into Chapter 4 for \nhands-on examples that demonstrate Terraform’s \ncapabilities.
