Thangamayan Saveetha School of Law, Saveetha Institute of Medical and Technical \nSciences, Chennai, India \nSaef Wbaid Department of Computers Techniques Engineering, College of Technical Engineering, The Islamic University, Najaf, Iraq \nKanchan Yadav Department of Electrical Engineering, GLA University, Mathura, India\n1A Comprehensive Overview of Graph \nConvolutional Network \nRiju Bhattacharya, Naresh Kumar Nagwani, Deepak Suresh Asudani, \nGurpreet Singh Chhabra, Sandhya Bhattacharya, and Sangeeta Kadam \n1.1 Introduction \nThe rapid growth of smart gadgets like smartphones, smart cars, and smart homes is \ncausing a tremendous increase in the amount of data sent across networks today [ 1]. Con-\ncurrently, a more complex network environment is created by expanding network services, \nimproving user experience, and utilizing technologies like network slicing, virtualization, \nand edge computing. An important obstacle for the expansion of networks in the future\nR. Bhattacharya (B) · G. S. Chhabra \nDepartment of CSE, GITAM School of Technology, GITAM Deemed to be University, \nVisakhapatnam, India \ne-mail: rbhattac2@gitam.edu \nG. S. Chhabra e-mail: \ngchhabra@gitam.edu \nN. K. Nagwani \nDepartment of CSE, National Institute of Technology, Raipur, India \ne-mail: nknagwani.cs@nitrr.ac.in \nD. S. Asudani \nSymbiosis Institute of Technology, Nagpur Campus, Symbiosis International (Deemed University), Pune, Nagpur, India \ne-mail: deepak.asudani@sitnagpur.siu.edu.in \nS. Bhattacharya \nDepartment of CS, Shri Shankaracharya Institute of Professional Studies, Raipur, India \ne-mail: sandhya.bhattacharya@ssipsraipur.in \nS. Kadam \nDepartment of CSE, SSIPMT, Raipur, India \ne-mail: s.kadam@ssipmt.com \n© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2026 \nR. Bhattacharya et al. (eds.), Graph Mining, Synthesis Lectures on Computer Science, \nhttps://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-93802-3_1 1\n2 R. Bhattacharya et al.\nis the effective control and management of numerous smart devices in complicated and \nlarge-scale network settings. Optimization and decision-making inside networks are aided \nby AI, which serves as the brain of future networks. At the same time, smart network cal-\nculations become possible when node computing capabilities are increased, which is like \nadding muscles and bones to the network [ 2]. Additionally, various complex and high-\ndimensional images produced from various data sources are contributing to the ongoing \nimprovement of the image processing area. \nA convolutional neural network (CNN) is a outstanding modelling abilities have con-\ntributed to its exponential rise in popularity in recent years. The ﬁelds of image processing and natural language processing, including machine translation, image recognition, and \nspeech recognition, among others, have made great strides since the advent of CNN com-\npared to earlier methods [\n3]. Data in areas where translation is not an issue, such as \nimages, text, and audio, are the exclusive purview of traditional convolutional neural networks. The ability to construct a convolutional neural network is made possible by \ntranslation invariance, which permits us to construct a globally shared convolution kernel in the input data space. By way of illustration, consider image data as a collection of uni-\nformly distributed pixels in Euclidean space; because to translation invariance, each pixel can serve as the centre of a locally identical structure of the same size [\n4]. This is the \nfoundation upon which the CNN builds accurate concealed layer illustrations of images \nthrough the modelling of local links and the learning of convolution kernels shared at \neach pixel. The conventional CNNs perform better in the picture and text domains, but \nthey are limited to working with data in the Cartesian coordinate system. Meanwhile, the \npervasiveness of non-Euclidean spatial data, often known as graph data, has been attract-\ning more and more attention. The networks in the real world, such transportation systems, \nthe Internet, and social media, can be organically represented using graph data. Because \ngraph data has a different local structure for each node than image and text data, transla-\ntion invariance is no longer satisﬁed [ 5]. One difﬁculty in deﬁning CNN on graph data is \nthe absence of translation invariance. Researchers have started to concentrate on ways to \nbuild deep-learning models on graphs in recent years, probably because graph data is so \nubiquitous. A prominent model is the graph neural network (GNN) [ 6] Beyond its inher-\nent beneﬁt in analyzing graph data, GNN’s explainability and effective application across \nreasoning tasks [ 7, 8] make it a theoretically and practically accessible approach. While \nthere are many different GNN models [ 9, 10] we focus mostly on graph convolutional \nnetworks (GCNs) [ 11], since they beat a lot of graph deep learning models at different \ngraph-based tasks. The most popular and inﬂuential approach now is Graph Convolutional \nNetworks (GCN), which uses CNNs’ capacity to simulate both local structures and the \npervasive graph dependencies. While a few publications have surfaced recently to analyse \nand summarize deep learning on graphs, there is still a lack of thorough coverage and \nsummaries of GCN’s modelling methods and applications, the most relevant branch.\n1 A Comprehensive Overview of Graph Convolutional Network 3\nGraph data, typical of non-Euclidean geographical data formats, displays multiple \ndependencies and interactions among its components [ 12]. Applying classic graph the-\nory methods to complicated graph problems in future networks will present considerable \nobstacles. Thus, a signiﬁcant scientiﬁc challenge in the domain of future networks is \nthe development of algorithms that can effectively address complex graph data, which \nwill govern the scheduling, administration, and allocation of communication network \nresources. Graph Neural Networks (GNNs) signify a revolutionary artiﬁcial intelligence \nmethodology that has created extensive possibilities for analyzing data using intricate \ngraph conﬁgurations. The swift topological information mining and superior feature \nextraction abilities of GCN, enhanced by AI technologies like deep learning and rein-\nforcement learning, have signiﬁcantly progressed knowledge graphs, computer vision, and \nrecommendation systems [ 1]. Consequently, efﬁciently and promptly tackling real-world \nissues requires an inventive integration of GCN with contemporary improvements [ 13]. \nIn recent years, several publications have been published to examine and describe deep \nlearning on graphs; however, the critical domain of Graph Convolutional Networks (GCN) requires further in-depth analysis and synthesis of its modelling techniques and implica-\ntions. This article meticulously organizes and summarizes the historical development and future directions of GCN, along with contemporary applications across several scientiﬁc \ndomains. The difﬁculties encountered in the creation of GCN primarily arise from the following factors: \n1. Graph data are spatial patterns that are not Euclidean: Every node in a graph has \na unique local structure because graph data, unlike non-Euclidean geographical data, \ndoes not adhere to translation invariance. Data translation invariance is the foundation \nof traditional convolutional neural networks’ fundamental operators, which include \nconvolution and pooling. Determining pooling and convolution operators for graph \ndata is currently a challenging task. \n2. Patterned graph data: The data in graphs has many uses in the real world and may \nillustrate a variety of uses, each with its own set of distinctive properties. For example, \ngraph data can be used to depict networks of social connections, citations, and political \nparties. There is a correlation between positive and negative patterns and many mark-\ners, such as signs and symbols. The increased variety of graph properties that GCNs \nmust model makes their design more challenging. \n3. Data from graphs on a massive scale: There are practical uses for graphs having millions of nodes. User commodity networks and social network user networks are \ntwo examples of such large-scale graphs. The challenge of building a large-scale graph convolutional neural network within appropriate time and space constraints is another \nmajor issue. \nThere are many different uses for GCN because graph-structured data is so common. \nMany different types of learning environments, including supervised, semi-supervised,\n4 R. Bhattacharya et al.\nunsupervised, and reinforcement learning, have investigated GCN. Nevertheless, GCN’s \npotential uses in computer vision and natural language processing are of primary interest \nto researchers. In order to infer document labels, GCN makes use of the relationships \nbetween documents or words. A syntax dependent tree is one example of an internal \ngraph structure that may be present in natural language data, which otherwise displays a \nsequential order. Using the word-relationship graph as an example, GCN is able to deal \nwith NLP issues. Learned from a semantic network of abstract words, graph-to-sequence \nlearning is another GCN application that can produce similar phrases. Generating scene \ngraphs, classifying point clouds, and recognizing actions are all uses of GCN in computer \nvision. \nThe complete taxonomy of graph convolutional Network has been presented in \nFig. 1.1. This chapter ﬁrstly introduces the basic model of GNN and importantly graph \nconvolutional neural networks; secondly, it introduces the speciﬁc methods of GCNs in \nvarious Fields of research such as NLP , computer vision etc.; in the conclusion part, it \ndiscusses the current research status and gives the future research direction.\n1.2 Background Study \nA revolutionary step forward in neural network technology, Graph Neural Networks (GNNs) are designed to handle graph-structured data with unparalleled precision. Graph \nneural networks (GNNs) are able to capture complex connections by making good use of the relationships between nodes using a novel message-passing mechanism. As a result of this noteworthy property, GNNs are able to greatly improve many different kinds of \ndata processing, including image processing, intelligent recommendation systems, and knowledge-based graphs. Welcome the possibility that GNNs may revolutionize the way \nwe examine and comprehend intricate data interactions. \nGeneral GNN Model \nThe GNN model was initially introduced by Gori et al. [ 14]. Its core components are the \nlocal transfer function and the local output function. The local transfer function generates a node’s state vector, incorporating neighborhood information. This transfer function is \nshared among all nodes, updating the node’s state vector \n(hv) based on its input neigh-\nborhood. The local output function then generates a new representation of the node [ 14]. \nThe local transfer function is essential as it generates the state vector of each node, encap-\nsulating valuable information about its neighbours. This function is consistently applied \nacross all nodes, ensuring a uniform updating process for the node’s state vector, referred \nto as h1. It adapts according to the input from the surrounding neighborhood, making it \na crucial component of the system. The expression for this transfer function is presented \nbelow:\n1 A Comprehensive Overview of Graph Convolutional Network 5\nFig. 1.1 An overview of a graph convolutional networks that represents its types and application \naeras\n6 R. Bhattacharya et al.\nhv = f(xv, xevu, hu, xu) \nwhere: hv is the state vector of node v, xv is the feature of node v, xevu is the feature \nconnecting node v and its neighbour node u, hu is the state vector of the neighbour node u, \nand xu is the feature of the neighbour node u. The local output function plays a crucial role \nin generating an enhanced representation of each node, as demonstrated in the following \nexpression: \nOv = g(hv, xv) \nwhere Ov is the output of node v, hv is the state vector of node v,a n d xv is the feature \nof node v. By effectively combining the local transfer function and the local output func-\ntion for all nodes, we establish a robust Graph Neural Network (GNN) structure. This \nmodel is designed to evolve and ultimately stabilize through iterative processes, ensuring \noptimal performance and accuracy. Early graph neural networks face major challenges, \nincluding inefﬁciency, high computational costs, and limited node characteristics. These \nissues hinder their ability to effectively inﬂuence the state of graphs after several updates. \nFortunately, recent advancements have introduced innovative graph neural networks and application studies that dramatically enhance the efﬁciency of processing graph-structured \ndata, paving the way for more powerful and effective solutions. \nGraph Convolutional Networks (GCNs) \nGraph Convolutional Networks (GCNs) are groundbreaking as they introduce a convo-\nlution operation tailored speciﬁcally for graph structures, making them one of the most \nvital types of Graph Neural Networks (GNNs) today. By employing distinct feature extrac-\ntion techniques, GCNs can be classiﬁed into those that leverage spectral-domain methods \nand those that harness spatial-domain approaches. Emerging from the principles of graph \nsignal processing, GCNs utilize ﬁlters to deﬁne graph convolution [ 15]. This innova-\ntive ﬁltering process effectively eliminates noise from the input signals, ensuring that \nwe obtain accurate and reliable classiﬁcation results. Assuming GCNs can signiﬁcantly \nenhance the performance of tasks involving complex graph data [ 16]. \nGraph Attention Networks (GANs) \nThe Graph Attention Network (GA T) revolutionizes the capabilities of Graph Convolu-\ntional Networks (GCN) by integrating an innovative attention mechanism. This powerful \nfeature empowers the model to prioritize the most pertinent information for the given \ntask, signiﬁcantly boosting performance. Traditional spectral domain-based GCNs rely \non a ﬁlter function tied to the Laplacian matrix derived from speciﬁc graph structures, \nlimiting their applicability to different graphs. Recognizing this limitation, V elikovi et al. \n[17] introduced GA T, a groundbreaking graph neural network architecture designed to \novercome these challenges. As illustrated in Fig. 1.2, the attention mechanism in GA T \nplays a crucial role in enhancing model efﬁciency and effectiveness.\n1 A Comprehensive Overview of Graph Convolutional Network 7\nFig. 1.2 Graph attention \nnetwork mechanism [ 17] \nThe attention mechanism in GA Ts calculates attention coefﬁcients between nodes and \ntheir neighbors9. The formula for calculating these coefﬁcients is \neij = a/parenleftbig\nWx i, Wx j/parenrightbig\nwhere W is the weight matrix applied to all nodes, representing the relationship between \ninput features and output features xi and xj are the features of node i and node j, \nrespectively. \nTo simplify operations, the attention coefﬁcients are regularized and used to generate \noutput features: \nx/prime\ni = σ ⎛ \n⎝/summationdisplay\nj∈N(i) αijWx j ⎞ ⎠ \nwhere σ(·) is the nonlinear activation function and αij is the regularized attention \ncoefﬁcient. \n1.3 Notations and Preliminaries \nThis section introduces key concepts and mathematical notations used in the chapter of \nGraph Convolutional Networks (GCNs). Here’s a summary: \n• Graphs and Graph Signals: \n– A graph is represented as G = {V , E, A}, where V is the set of nodes with |V| = n, E is the set of edges with |E| = m, and A is the adjacency matrix.\n8 R. Bhattacharya et al.\n– In the adjacency matrix A, A (i, j) denotes the weight of the edge between node i \nand node j; otherwise, A (i, j) = 0. For unweighted graphs, A (i, j) = 1. \n– The degree matrix D is a diagonal matrix where D (i, i) =/summationtextn \nj=1 = A(i, j). \n– The Laplacian matrix is denoted as L = D−A, and the symmetrically normalized \nLaplacian matrix is ˜L =I −D−1/2 A D−1/2, where I is an identity matrix, ˜Lr e p -\nresents the symmetrically normalized Laplacian matrix. D is the degree matrix, a \ndiagonal matrix where each diagonal element D (i, i) is the sum of the weights of \nall edges connected to node i. A is the adjacency matrix of the graph and D(−1/2) \nis the inverse square root of the degree matrix [ 18]. \n– A graph signal on the nodes is a vector X ∈ Rn, where x (i) is the signal value on \nnode i. Node attributes can be considered graph signals. X ∈ R(nxd) represents the \nnode attribute matrix of an attributed graph, with columns representing d signals of the graph. \nGraph Fourier Transform: \n• The graph Laplacian matrix L is the Laplace operator on a graph. An eigenvector of \nL associated with its eigenvalue is analogous to the complex exponential at a certain frequency. \n• The eigenvalue decomposition of ˜L is denoted as ˜L = U/Lambda1UT , where the lth column \nof U is the eigenvector U/lscriptand/Lambda1(/lscript, /lscript) is the corresponding eigenvalue λ/lscript. \n• The Fourier transform of a graph signal X is computed as ˆX (λ/lscript) =/angbracketleftX, U /lscript/angbracketright=\nn/summationtext\ni=1 X(i)U∗\n/lscript(i). \n• Graph Filtering: \nOne specialized method for processing graph signals is graph ﬁltering. His research interests include theories and practices of pedagogy, cul -\ntural and linguistic diversity, and new technologies of representation and \ncommunication. His and Mary Kalantzis’ recent research has focused on the \ndevelopment of digital writing and assessment technologies, with the support \nof a number of major grants from the US Department of Education, the Bill \nand Melinda Gates Foundation, and the National Science Foundation. The re -\nsult has been the CGScholar  multimodal writing and assessment environment. \nJohnathan Cruise  is a test development researcher with the English Lan -\nguage Research of the British Council in the United Kingdom, where he \nworks on various projects, primarily dynamic assessment in machine-driven \nlearning and young learner assessment literacy as well as standardization \nprojects. Before moving back to the UK in 2022, Johnathan managed a \npool of researchers in China as well as key initiatives in IELTS (Interna -\ntional English Language Testing System) and other assessment projects. \nJohnathan has over 15\xa0 years of experience as an examiner manager and \ntrainer and over 20\xa0years’ experience as a college teacher in business Eng -\nlish, English for Specific Purposes, English language teaching, and language \nteacher training. He was trained in state school general primary education \nin the UK and holds a master’s degree in applied linguistics and TESOL \n(Teaching English to Speakers of Other Languages). \nEmil Tangham Hazelhurst is an Associate Lecturer of corpus linguistics at \nLancaster University in the United Kingdom. He is currently participating \nin a research project  funded by the Economic and Social Research Council \nin collaboration with the British Council involving language testing and \nacquisition through the use of corpus techniques. He has published previ -\nously in the areas of language acquisition, corpus analysis, and ecocriti -\ncism and has written for The Palgrave Encyclopedia of Computer-Assisted \nLanguage Learning . He is also the co-editor of the upcoming collection \nApocalyptic Ecolinguistics: Language, Landscape, and Ecoanxiety in the Age \nof Ecological Crisis  with Bloomsbury. Zapata \nThe Transformative Potential of Generative Artificial \nIntelligence in Language Education \nIn their recent analysis of the possible impacts of artificial intelligence in edu -\ncation (AIED), Nguyen et al. (2022 , pp. 4221 and 4223) characterize AI as \n“one of the most pivotal developments of the century,” positing that it can be \n“seen as an influential tool to empower new paradigms of instruction.” Since \nthe launch of OpenAI’s ChatGPT in November 2022, interest in AIED, and, \nspecifically, in large language models (LLMs), has grown exponentially, as ed -\nucational researchers and practitioners have sought to understand the ways in \nwhich new Generative AI (GenAI) technologies might influence teaching and \nlearning, including language education ( Cope & Kalantzis, 2024 ; Kalantzis & \nCope, 2024 ). \nAs highlighted in recent systematic reviews and commentaries (e.g., \nChandel & Lim, 2024 ; Godwin-Jones, 2023 ; Law, 2024 ), GenAI has \nbrought transformative potential to second language (L2) education, spark -\ning discussions on how languages are taught and learned in an increasingly \ndigital and multimodal world. This volume seeks to contribute to these \ndiscussions by exploring the implications of GenAI for L2 teaching and \nlearning. Specifically, the chapters in this book examine GenAI’s alignment \nwith multiliteracies pedagogies and its capacity to support diverse learn -\ners in navigating and contributing to complex, multimodal communica -\ntion environments. By situating GenAI within the theoretical framework \nof multiliteracies as articulated by the New London Group (NLG, 1996) \nand Kalantzis et al. (2005 , 2016 , 2019 ), this volume underscores the criti -\ncal need for integrating these technologies thoughtfully and ethically into \neducational practices. \nIn this introduction, we jumpstart this exploration by addressing three key \nareas: The definition and capabilities of GenAI, current insights into its ap -\nplication in L2 education, and a review of the main tenets of multiliteracies \npedagogical approaches. We conclude the chapter outlining the contributions \nof this volume to advancing the field. \n2 Generative AI Technologies, Multiliteracies, & Language Education \nDefining GenAI \nGenAI refers to a class of artificial intelligence capable of creating diverse forms \nof content, including text, images, audio, video, and multimodal artifacts. \nCope and Kalantzis (2024)  describe GenAI as a combination of chatbot tech -\nnologies and LLMs, facilitating user interactions through prompts to generate \ntailored outputs. Unlike traditional AI applications that rely heavily on rule-\nbased systems, GenAI employs advanced, self-supervised learning techniques \nto analyze massive datasets, enabling it to predict contextually relevant next \nwords and produce coherent, adaptive responses (for an in-depth discussion \nof these technologies, see Chapter 1  in this volume). This paradigm shift has \nimplications across numerous domains, with education, and particularly L2 \neducation, emerging as a critical site of application ( Chandel & Lim, 2024 ; \nKalantzis & Cope, 2024 ). \nGenAI in L2 Education: Current Insights \nRecent compilations on existing research on GenAI and L2 education (e.g., \nChandel & Lim, 2024 ; Galaczi & Luckin, 2024 ; Law, 2024 ) have shown \nthat these technologies are transforming L2 teaching and learning, funda -\nmentally reshaping language instruction and literacy development. Tools like \nOpenAI’s ChatGPT  or Google’s Gemini  leverage advanced conversational \ncapabilities and personalized, multimodal outputs to create tailored learn -\ning experiences that address individual personal and linguistic needs. For \nexample, studies have highlighted GenAI’s beneficial effects for personalized \nwriting and vocabulary development (e.g., Boudouaia et al., 2024 ; Crum \net al., 2024 ; Escalante et al., 2023 ; Hwang et al., 2023 ; Javier & Moorhouse, \n2023 ; Karataş et al., 2024 ; Kostka & Toncelli, 2023 ; Mahapatra, 2024 ; \nPellas, 2023 ; Xiao & Zhi, 2023 ), as well as the effective implementation \nof formative assessment approaches such as dynamic assessment (e.g., Jeon, \n2021 ). Additionally, acting as a non-judgmental interlocutor, GenAI has \nbeen reported to alleviate learners’ anxiety, particularly in speaking and writ -\ning contexts where apprehension often hinders performance ( Song & Song, \n2023 ). Moreover, as evinced by Baidoo-Anu and Ansah’s (2023)  work, \nGenAI can facilitate the establishment of interactive, immersive scenarios \nthat simulate real-life conversations, enabling learners to develop practical \ncommunication skills in diverse contexts. Works that have explored students’ \nperceptions of GenAI-supported L2 learning (e.g., Abdelhalim, 2024 ; Javier \n& Moorhouse, 2023 ; Karataş et al., 2024 ; Xiao & Zhi, 2023 ) have also \nreported generally positive opinions, which appear to translate into higher \nlevels of motivation and overall instructional engagement. \nGenAI’s contributions extend beyond traditional literacy to supporting \nmultiliteracies, which include the ability to critically engage with and produce \nmultimodal texts. These tools can aid learners in integrating textual, visual, \nIntroduction 3 \nand auditory elements, enhancing their ability to communicate across var -\nied meaning forms. Interaction with GenAI also cultivates digital literacy by \nengaging students in activities such as prompt engineering and the evalu -\nation of AI-generated content ( Kalantzis & Cope, 2024 ). These processes \ncan develop critical thinking, problem-solving, and analytical skills, which are \nessential for navigating the complexities of the digital age ( Ng et al., 2021 ). \nGenAI’s integration into assessment represents another significant advantage, \nas it allows for ongoing, formative feedback embedded within learning pro -\ncesses. This approach facilitates individualized learning pathways, enabling \nteachers to focus on higher-order pedagogical tasks ( Godwin-Jones, 2023 ; \nKalantzis & Cope, 2024 ). \nDespite these benefits, the adoption of GenAI in L2 education also pre -\nsents challenges that require thoughtful mitigation. A major concern is the \nreliability of AI-generated outputs, as inaccuracies and biases can mislead \nlearners and perpetuate stereotypes ( Cope & Kalantzis, 2024 ; Nicoletti & \nBass, 2023 ). The opaque “black box” nature of many AI models further com -\nplicates their use, making it difficult for educators and students to understand \nhow outputs are generated ( Kalantzis & Cope, 2024 ). Ethical issues, such as \nacademic misconduct, plagiarism, privacy violations, and over-reliance on AI, \nhighlight the need for robust institutional guidelines and critical AI literacy \namong users ( Chandel & Lim, 2024 ; Galaczi & Luckin, 2024 ; Tzirides et al., \n2024 ). Research has also shown that excessive dependence on GenAI can \nstifle creativity and critical thinking, as learners may prioritize AI outputs \nover developing their original ideas ( Niloy et al., 2023 ). These risks are par -\nticularly pronounced in assessments, where undetectable use of AI-generated \ncontent threatens evaluation integrity, necessitating new methods to differen -\ntiate human effort from machine output ( Cope & Kalantzis, 2024 ; Kalantzis \n& Cope, 2024 ). \nThe studies previously presented have relied on diverse sources of data, \nincluding surveys, interviews, self-study logs, and experimental designs in -\ncorporating pre- and post-tests. This range of approaches has contributed \nto a broad understanding of how L2 learners engage with GenAI tools in \nlanguage classes. Nevertheless, much of this research has been conducted in \nan ad-hoc manner, lacking grounding in robust theoretical or pedagogical \nframeworks. For instance, the predominant reliance on the Technology Ac -\nceptance Model (TAM), as seen in studies such as those by Boudouaia et al. \n(2024)  and some of the works discussed in Law’s (2024)  literature review, \nhas resulted on information primarily connected to students’ acceptance and \nadoption of GenAI, mostly focused on ChatGPT. While TAM provides a \nuseful lens for examining technology adoption, it does not address the nu -\nanced pedagogical or theoretical considerations necessary for effective L2 \nteaching and learning. \nThese limitations highlight the need for research that is both theoretically \nand pedagogically grounded, such as studies informed by the pedagogy of \n4 Generative AI Technologies, Multiliteracies, & Language Education \nmultiliteracies. Incorporating this framework into GenAI-supported L2 edu -\ncation could provide a more comprehensive understanding of how this tech -\nnology can support students with diverse linguistic, academic, and personal \nneeds, fostering richer and more meaningful L2 learning experiences. The \nobjective of this volume is to address this need. But before we delve into its \ncontents, let’s revisit the pedagogy of multiliteracies. \nThe Pedagogy of Multiliteracies Revisited \nIn the mid-1990s, a group of scholars anticipated the profound changes in \neducation and communication that have become especially evident in recent \nyears. This group, known as the New London Group—a collective of ten \ninternational educators who, in 1994 convened in New London, New Hamp -\nshire, in the United States—focused on redefining literacy ( Cope & Kalantzis, \n2009 ; NLG, 1996). These scholars argued that traditional notions of literacy, \ncentered on printed language and standardized forms, were insufficient for \na generation increasingly engaged in multimodal and multilingual forms of \ncommunication. \nRecognizing the influence of globalization and technology, the NLG \n(1996) thus proposed a broader pedagogy of literacy. This pedagogy would \naccount for not only traditional printed language but also the diverse modali -\nties of communication prevalent in the contemporary world. Moreover, this \napproach would embrace learners’ diverse identities, experiences, and needs \n(Kalantzis et al., 2005). That is, for the NLG, literacy in a globalized, multi -\nmodal world required learners to actively engage with new and complex texts, \nfostering inclusivity rather than alienation. To address these societal changes \nand educational needs, the NLG introduced the pedagogy of multiliteracies , a \nframework that reflects the interconnectedness of social and modal dimensions \nin meaning-making ( Cope & Kalantzis, 2015 ). \nThe Concept of Multiliteracies \nBut what exactly did the NLG scholars (1996) have in mind when they \ncoined the term multiliteracies  in connection with the pedagogy they were \nproposing? What does the multi  in multiliteracies  refer to? Broadly speaking, \nthe term multiliteracies  makes reference to the multiple ways in which we \ncreate and convey meaning. These encompass two dimensions of meaning-\nmaking: the social  (context/function) and the modal  (form) ( Kalantzis et al., \n2016 , 2019 ). The first one is connected to the diverse social contexts in \nwhich communication takes places, which shape what and how we commu -\nnicate. The social multi might comprise the personal experiences, cultural or \n“community setting[s], social role[s], interpersonal relations, identit[ies], \nsubject matter, etc.” that are “significant to the ways in which we make and \nparticipate in meaning” ( Kalantzis et al., 2016 , pp. 1–2). The second dimen -\nsion, the modal , refers to the variety of communication meaning forms to \nIntroduction 5 \nwhich we might resort to create meaning, such as text, image, space, body, \nobject, sound, and/or speech ( Cope & Kalantzis, 2020 ). These forms are \ndirectly connected to the new media (and the tools and practices associ -\nated with them) which we experience daily, and we have come to rely on in \ntoday’s world. \nIn educational contexts, the pedagogy of multiliteracies seeks to prepare \nlearners to navigate and contribute to diverse, multimodal, and socially em -\nbedded forms of communication. It emphasizes the integration of multimodal \ntexts and technologies into curricula, reflecting varied social and literate prac -\ntices ( Anstey & Bull, 2006 ; Cope & Kalantzis, 2015 , 2023 ; Kalantzis et al., \n2005, 2016, 2019). Bull and Anstey (2019)  outline essential goals for such \nan approach: \n• Encourage learners to think strategically, creatively, and critically \n• Equip learners to adapt to different textual purposes, audiences, and contexts \n• Foster an understanding of the impact of social and cultural diversity on \nliteracy \n• Promote multimodal communicative competence with traditional and new \ncommunication technologies \n• Cultivate critical liter')
